Then, in courtyard 4, another child, 1 year and 2 months old, appeared in the same situation.\n\nAccording to the Personeria, as an immediate measure of protection, 17 pregnant women were transferred to courtyard 1, because it is a high-risk population, and quarantine measures were taken in patios 2, 4, and 6, to prevent the spread of the outbreak, while the children were sent with their external caretakers.\n\nIn the face of the situation, Personeria asked the health authorities to keep the inspections and sanitary actions to face the emergency.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[With a space of 11 days between the initial 2 cases, it is certainly possible that the 2nd child was exposed to the 1st. Varicella during pregnancy is particularly dangerous in the 3rd trimester with a substantial risk of varicella pneumonia in the pregnant woman.\n\nIn addition to the risk to the mother, congenital varicella syndrome may occur most frequently when maternal disease is acquired between 8-20 week gestation. The characteristic symptoms consist of skin lesions in dermatomal distribution (76%), neurologic defects (60%), eye diseases (51%), and skeletal anomalies (49%). However, a maternal infection beginning within 5 days of delivery is associated with development of neonatal varicella with a substantial mortality rate; the high death rate likely reflects inadequate passive immunity received from the mother in the short time period before birth. - Mod.LL\n\nHealthMap/ProMED-mail map of Colombia: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/55157]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n According to the Personeria, as an immediate measure of protection, 17 pregnant women were transferred to courtyard 1, because it is a high-risk population, and quarantine measures were taken in patios 2, 4, and 6, to prevent the spread of the outbreak, while the children were sent with their external caretakers. Two other children were subsequently infected.'\n\nCHEO says all safety precautions are in place, adding: 'The cases are contained, and the risk of transmission is extremely low.'\n\nFamilies whose children may have been exposed to chickenpox have been notified of the situation.\n\nAccording to the Ottawa Public Health website, chickenpox is caused by the varicella-zoster virus. It is a very common childhood infection that can also affect adults.\n\nThe health unit says: 'Chickenpox usually begins with a fever, followed in 1-2 days by a rash. The rash appears on the chest, back or stomach and spreads to other body areas. It usually starts with spots that soon become fluid-filled blisters. New spots appear over the next 2-3 days.'\n\nThe chickenpox virus can spread from person to person through the air, in saliva, or through direct contact with the fluid in a chickenpox blister. Chickenpox develops 10-21 days after a person has been exposed to the virus.\n\nOttawa Public Health says the best way to protect yourself and your family from chickenpox is by receiving the chickenpox vaccine. The vaccine is recommended for healthy children ages 12 months-12 years of age.\n\nIn a message on its website, CHEO tells parents: 'Most importantly, vaccines work. Please vaccinate your child for all preventable childhood infectious diseases.'\n\n[Byline: Josh Pringle]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Chickenpox is a highly infectious virus spread through direct contact with someone with the illness as well as through the air and in saliva. About 90 percent of people without immunity will come down with chickenpox if they have close contact with someone 1-2 days before they show symptoms of rash and until all of the chickenpox lesions have crusted. It usually takes about a week for all the blisters to scab over and no longer be infectious. Complications from chickenpox can occur, but they are not common in healthy people who get the disease. The chickenpox vaccine is a safe, effective way to prevent chickenpox and its possible complications. - Mod.LK\n\nHealthMap/ProMED maps:\nOntario Province, Canada: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/260\nOttawa, Ontario, Canada: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/277]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n \n\nThe chickenpox virus can spread from person to person through the air, in saliva, or through direct contact with the fluid in a chickenpox blister. Over half of those cases were found at the Alfa y Omega shelter. Agency staff found another 15 at the state's Family Development Office shelter and 8 more at the Hogar de Angeles shelter. Two additional cases were reported at Hijo Prodigo shelter.\n\nZazueta said 36 cases have been reported in Tijuana.\n\nAuthorities said a request was made to the Mexican government to distribute chickenpox vaccines. According to Zazueta, the government agreed to send 1000 vaccines that will be primarily given to minors who have not been vaccinated.\n\n[Byline: Arturo Bojorquez]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Given the close confinement of these refugees and the likelihood that the immunization schedule has not been completed, it is not surprising that diseases such as varicella will occur. Accumulations of susceptible individuals, such as military recruit camps and college campuses, experience periodic outbreaks of transmissible diseases including mumps and meningococcal infection. Although the medical information available related to the young people who have died in these camps is not really available, meningococcal disease has likely occurred.\n\nIt would be prudent that immunizations should be given. - Mod.LL\n\nHealthMap/ProMED map available at:\nMexico: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/14]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n Communicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Given the close confinement of these refugees and the likelihood that the immunization schedule has not been completed, it is not surprising that diseases such as varicella will occur. VARICELLA UPDATE (05): MEXICO, IMMIGRANTS\n*****************************************\n LL\n\nHealthMap/ProMED map available at:\nMexico: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/14] [Byline: Arturo Bojorquez]\n\n--\n \n\nState Chief Epidemiologist Oscar Zazueta said the number of Central American immigrants who have been confirmed with the illness has climbed to 51.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "mumps", "varicella"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-03-05 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "LL", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Tijuana", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Mexico", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "6290432", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Varicella update (01): USA (CO, VT)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=6290432", "date": "2019-02-01 18:23:08", "main_text": "VARICELLA UPDATE (01): USA (COLORADO, VERMONT)\n**********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nIn this update:\n[1] Colorado (Aurora)\n[2] Vermont (Randolph)\n\n******\n[1] Colorado (Aurora): ICE processing center\nDate: Thu 31 Jan 2019\nSource: Westword [abridged, edited]\nhttps://www.westword.com/news/second-chicken-pox-outbreak-at-aurora-immigrant-detention-center-puts-two-pods-in-quarantine-11210867\n\n\nThere has been another chicken pox [varicella] outbreak at the immigrant detention center in Aurora, the 2nd in just 3 months. A detainee 'pod,' which is a prison housing unit consisting of individual cells, was quarantined for weeks after an outbreak in October [2018].\n\nNow 2 pods have been quarantined for 21 days because of the virus, said GEO Group spokesman Pablo Paez in an e-mail. Paez did not answer additional questions about why a 2nd outbreak had occurred, or what the facility, which is managed by private prison company GEO Group through a contract with Immigration and Customs Enforcement (ICE), was doing to prevent future outbreaks.\n\nBut today, 31 Jan 2019, I [article author Chris Walker] met a detainee at the facility who described what it was like being under quarantine during last fall's outbreak. From behind a glass partition in the center's visitor room, [MA], age 34 years, described how guards one day told his pod, which housed 77 detainees, that they couldn't leave their housing unit, offering no explanation. 'We had no idea what was going on,' [MA] recalls. 'Guards just told us that we had to wait.'\n\nThey wound up waiting for a few days, during which they couldn't access the recreation yard, see visitors, consult face-to-face with lawyers, or even attend their immigration hearings. About a week in, the detainees finally learned that they were under a medical quarantine because of a possible chicken pox outbreak, [MA] says. The potentially sick individuals had been removed from the housing unit before it was locked down. And weeks into the quarantine, a doctor still had not visited the pod. Exasperated, [MA] and about 60 other detainees wrote letters to ICE and the GEO Group demanding answers -- and to see a doctor.\n\nHere's the letter:\n\n'It has been 2 weeks now that we have been in quarantine and not one doctor has been sent to this pod to offer medical treatment for the chicken pox. The first 3 people that were infected have been treated, cured and were switched over to a different pod, and are now living their normal process. But we have no visits, deportations, and our court [hearings] are being delayed.\n\n'They won't exchange our blankets, give haircuts, and we are prohibited from having rec time in the yard. We are being exposed to dangerous medical conditions living like this, causing depression, anxiety, high blood pressure, and conditions that could be fatal.\n\n'The most difficult part is the court dates being rescheduled. Many of us have already had chicken pox or been vaccinated. Our immigration process is being delayed because this outbreak wasn't properly handled from the start, and it's not fair. It feels like medical resources don't want to be used on us because we don't deserve it. We are all in desperate need of help.'\n\nA doctor finally visited the pod, dressed in a full-body hazmat suit 'looking like an astronaut,' says [MA].\n\nSome of the information that [MA] provided about the October 2018 outbreak (at least the number of individuals who were quarantined) runs counter to what ICE had told media outlets, including Westword, at the time. 'Of the 77 detainees who were tested, medical staff diagnosed 3 detainees with varicella; 7 others had low immunity and therefore possessed increased risk factors of contracting the disease; all 10 were quarantined at the facility,' spokesman Carl Rusnok said in a statement. 'The Aurora medical staff continues to provide high-level care to all those affected, while at the same time continuing to serve the medical needs of the entire facility population.'\n\nMultiple requests for comment sent to ICE about the 2nd outbreak haven't been returned; if and when they are, we'll update this story.\n\n[Byline: Chris Walker]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This situation is unacceptable and entirely preventable. Why were all residents not vaccinated during the outbreak in October 2018? The incubation period is 10 to 21 days. Will they be released from quarantine after this time period? Disease spreads easily when people are housed in overcrowded tight quarters, such as the situation at these detention centers. Many people who cross the border already need treatment for diseases (like chickenpox, tuberculosis), and sanitation is a big problem. These pathogens are highly transmissible. Vaccines need to be administered, and more individual medical attention is needed to prevent exposure of the group to an infectious individual.\n\nUS Immigration and Customs Enforcement (ICE), the Department of Homeland Security agency that operates detention centers, states on its website that:\n1. Residents will have access to health care education and maintenance services that are determined by the health care authority to be necessary and appropriate. Services will include prevention, diagnosis, and treatment of medical, dental, and mental health conditions.\n2. Newly admitted residents will be informed how to access health services, in a language they can understand.\n3. Residents will be able to initiate requests for health services.\n4. Residents will have access to the care determined necessary by the health care authority from a resident's admission to the residential facility until they are discharged from treatment, transferred to another facility, or removed from the US. When indicated, care shall include referral to community-based providers (https://www.ice.gov/detention-standards/family-residential). - Mod.LK\n\nHealthMap/ProMED-mail map:\nColorado, United States: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/209]\n\n******\n[2] Vermont\nDate: Wed 30 Jan 209\nSource: Valley News [abridged, edited]\nhttps://www.vnews.com/Vermont-Technical-College-Has-One-Confirmed-Case-of-Chickenpox-23142069\n\n\nVermont Technical College warned members of its community of one confirmed case of chickenpox in a student on its Randolph campus on Wednesday [30 Jan 2019].\n\nThe disease, known for its blister-like rash, is very contagious, so the college -- which in Randolph has about 400 residential students and about 700 students in total -- is urging members of its campus community to be diligent about handwashing, said Amanda Chaulk, the college's director of marketing and communications.\n\nShe said she hopes that those who are at greater risk, such as those with compromised immune systems or those who are pregnant, take note of this information. 'They may elect to not return to campus for a period of time,' Chaulk said.\n\nWhile complications from chickenpox can occur, most healthy people who get the disease will recover in 5 to 7 days, Ben Truman, spokesman for the Vermont Department of Health, wrote in an e-mail. In addition to the rash, common symptoms include fever, fatigue, loss of appetite and headache.\n\nMost people are immune to chickenpox due to previous childhood vaccination or history of disease, Truman said. But college officials will review students' immunization records and contact those who might be at risk, according to a health alert on the college's website.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[HealthMap/ProMED-mail map:\nVermont, United States: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/367]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n Communicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[HealthMap/ProMED-mail map:\nVermont, United States: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/367] LK\n\nHealthMap/ProMED-mail map:\nColorado, United States: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/209]\n\n******\n Communicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[This situation is unacceptable and entirely preventable. \n\nThe disease, known for its blister-like rash, is very contagious, so the college -- which in Randolph has about 400 residential students and about 700 students in total -- is urging members of its campus community to be diligent about handwashing, said Amanda Chaulk, the college's director of marketing and communications. if and when they are, we'll update this story.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "tuberculosis", "varicella"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2019-01-31 00:00:00", "0209-01-30 00:00:00", "2021-03-01 00:00:00", "2019-01-30 00:00:00", "2021-03-31 00:00:00", "2021-10-25 00:00:00", "2018-10-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Aurora", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Randolph", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Vermont", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "US", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Colorado", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "United States", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "6489393", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Varicella update (04): USA (CA) student", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=6489393", "date": "2019-05-27 12:46:09", "main_text": "VARICELLA UPDATE (04): UNITED STATES (CALIFORNIA) STUDENT\n*********************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Wed 22 May 2019\nSource: Mustang News [abridged, edited]\nhttp://mustangnews.net/full-vaccinations-are-required-by-university-immunization-policy-but-a-student-was-still-diagnosed-with-chicken-pox/\n\n\nAccording to California State University (CSU) Immunization Requirements (Executive Order [EO] 803), students are permitted to attend a CSU for up to an entire academic year before providing proof of full immunization upon the campuses' discretion.\n\nUniversity spokesperson Matt Lazier wrote in an email to Mustang News that the CSU immunization policy requires proof of measles, mumps, and rubella (MMR) vaccines within 1 year of enrolment and for students who are 18 years old or younger when starting at a university must show proof of hepatitis B immunization. Lazier wrote that Cal Poly follows these rules. Religious exemptions and exemptions for distance learning students are allowed.\n\nOn 5 Mar 2019, a Cal Poly student was diagnosed with varicella, otherwise known as chickenpox. The varicella vaccine is not one of the required vaccinations, according to Lazier. Only the MMR and hepatitis B vaccines are required.\n\n'Students who do not provide proof of full immunization by the beginning of the 2nd term of the academic year (that is, fall, winter, spring) shall not be allowed to enrol in future classes until proof of full immunization has been provided to the campus,' the Cal Poly Immunization Policy reads.\n\nHow a student may bypass vaccinations past their 2nd term\n---------------------------------------------------------\nIn January of 2016, Governor Jerry Brown passed a bill titled 'SB 277', which banned the 'personal or religious belief' vaccination exemption for schools. Currently, students can be exempt from immunization if forms are submitted by a physician stating that it would be medically unsafe for the individual to be immunized. After Brown's bill passed, the rate of medical exemptions nearly quadrupled, according to a study by the Journal of Pediatrics.\n\nCurrently, the CSU-wide immunization policy is under revision. According to Lazier the revised EO 803 will go into effect in the fall of 2020. Because the EO 803 is not yet revised, all students who either received vaccinations or were exempt while attending California Public School have satisfied the requirement to be admitted into the CSU System. 'Students enrolled in a California Public School for the 7th grade or higher on or after 1 Jul 1999 will have satisfied this requirement,' Chancellor Charles B Reed wrote in the EO 803.\n\nIn an immunization requirement form posted on Cal Poly's website, the form states that 'students who graduated from a California Public high school after January 2005 do not need to complete and submit this form to provide proof of immunization against measles, mumps, rubella, and hepatitis B.' However, due to the permissible 'personal belief' exemption that was in place prior to 2016, there is no promise that all public school kids were vaccinated, according to County of San Luis Obispo Public Health Department Health Agency communications coordinator Tara Kennon. 'While the state public health department and local school systems would have recommended that people who attended public school in [California] receive immunizations, personal belief exemptions were allowed until 2016 -- so there was no guarantee that all public school students would be vaccinated,' Kennon wrote.\n\nData collected from the California Department of Public Health concluded that in San Luis Obispo county the overall average percentage of students up to date on their vaccines in the 7th grade public school system was 96.78%. The county-wide private school system's percentages were significantly lower, at 89.8% up to date in the 2017-2018 school year. In order to prevent measles from spreading, 90-94% of people must be vaccinated in order to achieve 'herd immunity.'\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Varicella vaccination is not one of the required immunizations to enrol at CSU and Cal Poly; only MMR and hepatitis are actually required. And proof of vaccination with MMR and hepatitis vaccines is not required for up to one year following entry at CSU. The Executive Order currently is being revised and will be in place in 2020, but there is no indication varicella will be included. Medical exemptions to the MMR and hepatitis vaccines is allowed, but unfortunately, after passage of the bill disallowing personal and religious exemptions in 2016 in California, medical exemptions quadrupled (See Mohanty S, Buttenheim AM, Joyce CM, et al. Experiences with medical exemptions after a change in vaccine exemption policy in California. Pediatrics. 2018; 142(5): e20181051; http://pediatrics.aappublications.org/cgi/pmidlookup?view=long&pmid=30373910). This study concludes that 'without additional legal changes, including a standardized review of medical exemptions, some physicians may continue to write medical exemptions for vaccine hesitant parents.' This is a hurdle to achieving 95% immunization rates to provide herd immunity. As a matter of note, meningococcal immunization is also voluntary. - Mod.LK\n\nHealthMap/ProMED-mail map of California: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/204.]", "summary": "\n\n'Students who do not provide proof of full immunization by the beginning of the 2nd term of the academic year (that is, fall, winter, spring) shall not be allowed to enrol in future classes until proof of full immunization has been provided to the campus,' the Cal Poly Immunization Policy reads. \n\nUniversity spokesperson Matt Lazier wrote in an email to Mustang News that the CSU immunization policy requires proof of measles, mumps, and rubella (MMR) vaccines within 1 year of enrolment and for students who are 18 years old or younger when starting at a university must show proof of hepatitis B immunization. \n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Varicella vaccination is not one of the required immunizations to enrol at CSU and Cal Poly; only MMR and hepatitis are actually required. While the state public health department and local school systems would have recommended that people who attended public school in [California] receive immunizations, personal belief exemptions were allowed until 2016 -- so there was no guarantee that all public school students would be vaccinated,' Kennon wrote. In an immunization requirement form posted on Cal Poly's website, the form states that 'students who graduated from a California Public high school after January 2005 do not need to complete and submit this form to provide proof of immunization against measles, mumps, rubella, and hepatitis B.' Medical exemptions to the MMR and hepatitis vaccines is allowed, but unfortunately, after passage of the bill disallowing personal and religious exemptions in 2016 in California, medical exemptions quadrupled (See Mohanty S, Buttenheim AM, Joyce CM, et al. A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n According to California State University (CSU) Immunization Requirements (Executive Order [EO] 803), students are permitted to attend a CSU for up to an entire academic year before providing proof of full immunization upon the campuses' discretion. \n\nData collected from the California Department of Public Health concluded that in San Luis Obispo county the overall average percentage of students up to date on their vaccines in the 7th grade public school system was 96.78%. However, due to the permissible 'personal belief' exemption that was in place prior to 2016, there is no promise that all public school kids were vaccinated, according to County of San Luis Obispo Public Health Department Health Agency communications coordinator Tara Kennon. ' This study concludes that 'without additional legal changes, including a standardized review of medical exemptions, some physicians may continue to write medical exemptions for vaccine hesitant parents.' Because the EO 803 is not yet revised, all students who either received vaccinations or were exempt while attending California Public School have satisfied the requirement to be admitted into the CSU System. said yesterday [Tue 19 Feb 2019].\n\nThe figure was higher than the previous 3 weeks and the highest reported during the same week in the past 5 years, the centers said, adding that most of those diagnosed with the disease were children and adolescents between 5 and 14 years old.\n\n'Since the chicken pox vaccine became included in the nation's recommended routine immunizations for infants and children in 2004, the incidence rate has fallen by nearly 9%, but clustered cases still sometimes occur on school campuses,' CDC Deputy Director-General Philip Lo said.\n\nA total of 5 clusters were reported in the past 4 weeks, while 11 clusters out of the 12 reported this year [2019] were in schools, 6 of which were elementary schools, he said.\n\n'About 10 to 20% of elementary-school students who were vaccinated against chicken pox at a younger age gradually lose protective immunity over time,' Lo said. Although specialists believe that it is not necessary to revaccinate everyone, the centers still encourage parents of children who have lost protective immunity to consider getting their children revaccinated, he added.\n\nAs the chicken pox is highly contagious and can transmit through air or fluids, the centers urged people experiencing symptoms -- fever, loss of appetite, headaches, tiredness, malaise, itchy rashes and blisters -- to wear a surgical mask, see a doctor immediately and avoid going to school.\n\nMeanwhile, 161 019 people last week [11-17 Feb 2019] visited hospitals and clinics complaining of diarrhea, up 9.2% from the prior week [4-10 Feb 2019], the centers said. In the past 4 weeks, 25 clustered cases have been reported from mainly restaurants and hotels, it added.\n\nLate winter and early spring are the common peak season for chicken pox and diarrhea, it said, urging people to wash their hands and practice good cough etiquette, as well as rest at home until at least 48 hours after their symptoms have subsided.\n\n[Byline: Lee I-chia]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n******\n[2] Colorado (Aurora): ICE facility\nDate: Wed 20 Feb 2019\nSource: Fox31 and Channel 2 news [edited]\nhttps://kdvr.com/2019/02/20/u-s-rep-crow-demands-answers-about-possible-chickenpox-outbreak-at-aurora-ice-facility/\n\n\nDemocratic Rep. As of Fri 16 Nov 2018, 36 students at Asheville Waldorf School had contracted the varicella virus, known to most as chickenpox. The school has one of the highest vaccination religious exemption rates in the state.\n\n'People don't think it's a serious disease, and for the majority of people it's not. But it's not that way for everybody,' Mullendore said. Two to 3 out of every 1000 children infected with chickenpox required care in a hospital, she said.\n\nEach year, the chickenpox vaccine prevents more than 3.5 million cases of varicella, 9000 hospitalizations and 100 deaths, the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention states on its webpage. That's why health care providers for years have recommended all children medically able, namely those who have healthy immune systems, be vaccinated, Mullendore said.\n\nThose recommendations have, by and large, gone unheeded by the parents of Asheville Waldorf's 152 students, 110 of whom have not received the chickenpox vaccine, which was made available in the United States in 1995.\n\nNorth Carolina's Department of Health and Human Services tracks the rate of kindergarten attenders whose parents have claimed a religious exemption, allowing them to forego vaccination. During the 2017-2018 school year, the last for which data were available, Asheville Waldorf had a higher rate of religious exemptions for vaccination than all but 2 other schools in the state. Of the 28 kindergarten attenders who enrolled that year, 19 had an exemption to at least one vaccination required by the state for school entry.\n\nSchool officials responded to questions from the Citizen Times on Fri 16 Nov 2018. The school enrols students from nursery age through 6th grade, Mullendore said.\n\nThe only 2 North Carolina schools to top Asheville Waldorf's religious exemption rate were private schools in other counties. Both had 100 per cent exemption rates; one had only one kindergarten student, the other had 2. During the same academic year, Buncombe County led the state in religious exemption rates for kindergartners with 5.7 per cent.\n\n'The thing people need to understand is that when you have pockets of unvaccinated people, they serve as reservoirs for disease,' said Susan Sullivan, a nurse with the state DHHS who consults with local health departments about vaccines and preventable diseases.\n\nBuncombe County has long dealt with high vaccination exemption rates, in part from parents who fear vaccines more than the illnesses they prevent.\n\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail rapporteur Mary Marshall\n\n[See: ProMED-mail. In an interview on Wednesday [20 Mar 2019], Dr. Patricia Grace Trabado, PHO head, said the cases were observed as patients who sought treatment at the rural health units (RHU) and private clinics in Tubungan.\n\nAll the cases were recorded in Cadabdab Elementary School, with 21 male and 15 female students affected. Trabado said affected pupils might still be attending school even though they were infected, which resulted in its transmission.\n\nShe emphasized that the spread of the infection might have been prevented if the children were advised not to attend school with the onset of infection. 'If a child is showing chickenpox symptoms, especially when he or she was previously exposed to an affected person, then the pupil will be advised to stay at home,' she said.\n\nTrabado said the source of the infection came from the 1st patient working in Iloilo City but lives in Tubungan town. 'The patient might have a family member that attends school in Cadabdab. From there, we see where the infection originated,' she said. Trabado, however, did not give figures of the disease other than that from the school.\n\nThe RHUs and private clinics were able to monitor and manage the cases, Trabado said. All the student patients were discharged, given medication, and let the viral infection take its course. 'And eventually, the patients recovered,' she said. However, Trabado warns that a child with chickenpox can get secondary infection when he or she scratches the blisters, creating skin lesions.\n\nShe added that cases of chickenpox and mumps are mostly observed during the summer season. Trabado said patients with chickenpox experience fever and headache in the first 1 or 2 days before the itchy blister rash appears.\n\n[Byline: Gail Momblan]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[HealthMap/ProMED-mail map:\nPhilippines: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/158]\n\n******\n[2] New Mexico\nDate: Fri 22 Mar 2019\nSource: CBS 4 Local [edited]\nhttps://cbs4local.com/news/local/some-prisoners-quarantined-in-otero-county-prison-to-stop-spread-of-chickenpox\n\n\nHealth officials at a prison in southern New Mexico are working to stop the spread of chickenpox. that in late October [2018] started at a private school isn't over, according to [Buncombe] county health officials, who say the virus has sickened people outside of the school.\n\nIn addition to the 37 Asheville Waldorf School students who contracted chickenpox in the weeks following the 1st case, 4 people in the community have become ill, according to the Buncombe County Department of Health and Human Services.\n\nState health officials have deemed this North Carolina's worst chickenpox outbreak since the mid-1990s, when a vaccine for the virus went into circulation.\n\nThe onset of rash in the most recent Asheville case occurred [Mon 26 Nov 2018], said Buncombe health department spokeswoman Stacey Wood.\n\nWood would not say whether the 4 cases outside of Asheville Waldorf School were reported in parents or the friends of sickened students.\n\nThere are no longer any pending quarantine orders, the last of which expired on 20 Nov 2018, she said.\n\nThe health department, acting in accordance with the rules set by the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, will not declare Asheville's chickenpox outbreak finished until 42 days after the onset of the most recent case.\n\nThis chickenpox outbreak drew national attention as the West Asheville private school became the battleground for debate over the efficacy of vaccines.\n\nRoughly 75 percent of the school's 152 students -- ranging in age from 2 to 12 -- hadn't received a varicella vaccine. Varicella is the virus responsible for both chickenpox and shingles.\n\nBarring 2 outliers, Asheville Waldorf had the highest percentage of incoming kindergartners during 2017 whose parents had claimed religious exemption from one or more mandatory vaccine.\n\nIn North Carolina, a religious exemption can be obtained with relative ease. There is no official paperwork. State statute, however, doesn't allow parents to forgo vaccination for their children based on their personal beliefs.\n\nAfter county health officials learned of the early chickenpox cases at Asheville Waldorf School in the final days of October [2018], the Buncombe health department issued a quarantine order to 104 students.\n\n[Byline: Sam DeGrave]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Kunihiko Iizuka\n\n[It appears only 25% of the school's children are vaccinated with measles, mumps, rubella (MMR) vaccine, which creates a very dangerous public health situation. The unvaccinated children were quarantined for 3 weeks to protect them from infection. A judge struck down a portion of the quarantine order, ruling the county had the authority to keep children out of school but couldn't prevent them from leaving home. -Mod.LK]\n\nHealthMap/ProMED-mail map of North Carolina, United States: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/5719\nNorth Carolina county map: https://www.mapsofworld.com/usa/states/north-carolina/maps/north-carolina-county-map.jpg. There are 5 confirmed cases of varicella at the campus, Battle Ground Public Schools spokeswoman Rita Sanders said Friday [19 Oct 2018].\n\nClark County Public Health requires that students and staff who do not have documented immunity against the virus must stay home for 3 weeks beginning Monday [22 Oct 2018], according to a letter sent to parents on Thursday night [18 Oct 2018]. Individuals who get a vaccine or provide proof of immunity can return before then.\n\nThat's expected to keep 38 students out of class, Sanders said. There are 541 students at Daybreak Primary School.\n\nA higher percentage of students are exempt from vaccines at the north-county school than the county and state as a whole, according to the most recent data from the Department of Health. In the 2017-2018 school year, 13.2 percent of students were exempted from at least one vaccine. The majority, 11 percent, fell under the broad umbrella of personal vaccine exemptions. For varicella specifically, 11.4 percent of students were exempt from the vaccine.\n\nAcross Clark County, 7.5 percent of students are vaccine-exempt, 5.9 percent have personal exemptions, and 6 percent are exempt for the varicella vaccine. Statewide, the numbers are smaller still: 4.9 percent total, 3.7 percent personal, and 3.7 percent for varicella.\n\nFamilies and staff can provide proof of vaccination by providing the district any of the following:\n\n- Proof of date of birth before 1980\n- Record of at least one dose of the chickenpox vaccine verified by a licensed healthcare provider\n- A letter from a licensed healthcare provider documenting infection with and full recovery from previous chickenpox illness\n- A letter from a licensed healthcare provider that confirms a blood test or serology showing chickenpox immunity.\n\nChickenpox is caused by the highly contagious varicella zoster virus, according to Clark County Public Health. The illness is marked by an itchy rash with pink spots and tiny fluid-filled blisters, the eponymous pox. The average infected person develops between 300 and 400 pox on their body. An infected person may also have a fever and be generally fatigued. The illness can be spread through secretions like saliva, contact with a pox, or through the respiratory system.\n\nFamilies who believe their child is ill with chickenpox are asked to inform the school and seek medical care.\n\n[Byline: Katie Gillespie]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Chickenpox is normally a mild disease, but it can be serious and can lead to complications or death, especially in high-risk people. Complications include bacterial infections of the skin, soft tissues, bones, joints, or bloodstream (sepsis); dehydration; pneumonia; inflammation of the brain (encephalitis); toxic shock syndrome; and Reye's syndrome for people who take aspirin during chickenpox.\n\nWho's at risk?\nThose at high risk of having complications from chickenpox include newborns and infants whose mothers never had chickenpox or the vaccine; adults; pregnant women who haven't had chickenpox; people whose immune systems are impaired by medication, such as chemotherapy, or another disease, such as cancer or HIV; people who are taking steroid medications for another disease or condition, such as children with asthma; and people taking drugs that suppress their immune systems (excerpted from https://www.mayoclinic.org/diseases-conditions/chickenpox/symptoms-causes/syc-20351282).\n\nFor these reasons, chicken pox parties are not advised. Vaccination according to protocol is the best and safest preventative measure. - Mod.LK\n\nHealthMap/ProMED-mail map:\nClark County, Washington, United States: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/4647]", "summary": "\n\nFamilies and staff can provide proof of vaccination by providing the district any of the following:\n\n- Proof of date of birth before 1980\n- Record of at least one dose of the chickenpox vaccine verified by a licensed healthcare provider\n- A letter from a licensed healthcare provider documenting infection with and full recovery from previous chickenpox illness\n- A letter from a licensed healthcare provider that confirms a blood test or serology showing chickenpox immunity. Up to 39 suspected patients have been found in 48 hours. Among them, 27 are students of Skill Development Center located at Dimna Resort Complex and 12 Golmuri Police Lines personnel. Given the growing number of chickenpox patients, the health department has issued a public instruction that if a large number of people suffer from red spots, fever etc. in any area, then inform the department.\n\nConfirmation of 7 in one month: According to the report received from the district surveillance department, chicken pox has been confirmed in 7 people in the district for one month. Ten days ago, the team of Health Department took blood samples of 25 soldiers after receiving information about the spread of chicken pox in the Golmuri police line. After investigation, 7 of these policemen were confirmed with chickenpox.\n\nIt is important that you do not take any medicines without any medical advice, because there may be side effects of wrong medication. If possible, take chickenpox vaccine. After getting the vaccine, the possibility of getting this disease disappears for 40 years.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n\n[Infection with varicella zoster virus (VZV; also known as chickenpox virus), a human herpesvirus, occurs worldwide, and most persons living in temperate regions become immune by contracting varicella illness or receiving vaccinations by early adulthood. However, in tropical climates, as little as half the population is exposed to VZV by adulthood. The extent of disease and severity in adults in tropical climates is also greater than for temperate climates. In India, >30 percent of persons >15 years of age are susceptible. Routine infant vaccination has substantially reduced transmission of wild-type varicella; however, VZV vaccine is not a part of the Indian Universal Immunisation Program .\nExcerpted from J Meyers et al. Epidemic Varicella Zoster Virus among University Students, India. Emerg Infec Dis. 24 (2) --February 2018 https://wwwnc.cdc.gov/eid/article/24/2/17-0659_article\n\nSee Dispatch for full article and references.\n\nJamshedpur is the largest city in the state of Jharkhand. According to the report received from the district surveillance department, chicken pox has been confirmed in 7 people in the district for one month. Later, a rash on her skin made her feel like small needles were stabbing her.\n\n[She] works for CTV News and she has experienced the pain caused by shingles. She said that she couldn't walk for a few days from the nerve pain: 'It was in my back and then traveled down my leg as well, so sitting all day was extremely uncomfortable and I had some trouble sleeping at night.'\n\nWhen she went to visit her doctor, he said that she had shingles. But the disease is less common in young people, and [she] is 36 years old.\n\nEach year in Canada, there are 130 000 people that develop the disease. The Canadian researchers and the US CDC have noticed that in the last few years, the numbers have increased, but they don't know why.\n\nPeople that had chickenpox [are at risk of developing shingles], because the varicella-zoster virus that caused chickenpox can remain dormant in the body, even if chickenpox cleared. The virus remains until the immunity system wears it off. In some cases, the virus reactivates as shingles, which is a painful rash that looks like a stripe on the body, usually on the trunk. It's a mystery why the virus reactivates only in some people, but the risks increase with age.\n\nSome young people can develop shingles because their immune system is weak or they are under high levels of stress.\n\nIt's important to check a medic, says Dr Lynora Saxinger, infectious diseases expert at the University of Alberta Hospital: 'If you start treatment for it, you can reduce the severity and reduce the risk of going on to a pain syndrome that can sometimes be prolonged. You can actually improve your chances of healing well by getting therapy early.'\n\nThere are 10-15 percent of people with shingles that develop severe pain where the rash occurs, with a pain that can go for weeks and months. To shorten the illness and to prevent the pains in the postherpetic neuralgia (PHN), Dr Saxinger said that patients receive antiviral medications.\n\nHowever, shingles could be triggered by other diseases:\n\n'Shingles in a younger person could be a sign of another underlying health condition. So if you get shingles, it might be worth having a check-up to make sure you don't have another health condition that hasn't been recognized.'\n\n[Byline: Rex Austin]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Infection with the varicella-zoster virus leads to chickenpox, or primary varicella. The virus then lies dormant and can later reactivate as shingles, or herpes zoster. it presents as a painful rash that usually develops on one side of the body, often the face or torso. The rash consists of blisters that typically scab over in 7-10 days and clears up within 2-4 weeks. Some people describe the pain as an intense burning sensation. For some people, the pain can last for months or even years after the rash goes away. This long-lasting pain is called postherpetic neuralgia (PHN), and it is the most common complication of shingles. Your risk of getting shingles and PHN increases as you get older.\n\nA new shingles vaccine called Shingrix was licensed by the US Food and Drug Administration (FDA) in 2017. CDC recommends that healthy adults 50 years and older get 2 doses of Shingrix, 2-6 months apart. Shingrix provides strong protection against shingles and PHN. Shingrix is the preferred vaccine, over Zostavax\u00ae, a shingles vaccine in use since 2006.\n\nTwo doses of Shingrix provide strong protection against shingles and postherpetic neuralgia.\n- In adults 50-69 years old who got 2 doses, Shingrix was 97 percent effective in preventing shingles; among adults 70 years and older, Shingrix was 91 percent effective.\n- In adults 50-69 years old who got 2 doses, Shingrix was 91 percent effective in preventing PHN; among adults 70 years and older, Shingrix was 89 percent effective.\nShingrix protection remained high (more than 85 percent) in people 70 years and older throughout the 4 years following vaccination. Since your risk of shingles and PHN increases as you get older, it is important to have strong protection against shingles in your older years.\n(excerpted from https://www.cdc.gov/shingles/vaccination.html)\n\nZostavax [the former vaccine that has been around since 2006] only lowers the odds of getting shingles by 51 percent, and of PHN by 67 percent. It's even less effective in people ages 70 and older. The Zostavax vaccine contains a live but weakened version of varicella zoster -- the virus that causes shingles and chickenpox.\n\nShingrix [the new vaccine] is a killed version of the zoster virus. It also contains an adjuvant. 'It causes your immune system to produce more antibodies to fight shingles than Zostavax produced. So your body has a stronger immune response to the Shingrix vaccine than to the Zostavax vaccine,' says Michael Hogue, PharmD, a professor of pharmacy at the Samford University College of Health Sciences.\n\n[As a result,] Shingrix causes more side effects than Zostavax. The main side effects reported in studies were soreness, redness, and swelling where you get the shot. Some people also got headaches or felt tired or achy after the shot. About 1 out of 10 people said the side effects were severe enough to disrupt their daily life. Yet most felt better within 3 to 5 days.\n\n'The side effects of the Shingrix are temporary and usually last 2 to 3 days. While you may experience pain for a few days after getting Shingrix, the pain will be less severe than having shingles and the complications from the disease,' the CDC says.\n(excerpted from https://www.webmd.com/skin-problems-and-treatments/shingles/news/20180212/new-shingles-vaccine_what-you-need-to-know). - Mod.LK\n\nHealthMap/ProMED-mail map\nCanada: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/12]", "summary": "In adults 50-69 years old who got 2 doses, Shingrix was 97 percent effective in preventing shingles; among adults 70 years and older, Shingrix was 91 percent effective. In adults 50-69 years old who got 2 doses, Shingrix was 91 percent effective in preventing PHN; among adults 70 years and older, Shingrix was 89 percent effective. At government facilities, the number is higher with the per-week count of patients often touching 15. According to authorities, while the 1st cases of chicken pox, this year [2018], started to surface almost 2 months ago, the numbers are now rising steadily.\n\n'Though we do get cases throughout the year, the rate of occurrence is maximum between March and May. We are getting about 5 cases every week now,' said Dr Vikram Simha Reddy, consultant physician, Century Super Specialty Hospitals. ...-more\n\nUsually, it is a childhood disease but can occur at any age. Chicken pox is a contagious viral disease caused by the Varicella Zoster Virus that spreads through direct contact with the infected person. Patients are usually infected from 2 days before the rash appears to 5 days after the rash and are instructed to avoid contact with anyone during this period. 'The earlier you start the treatment, the slower is the progression of rashes. The start of antivirals also helps in preventing major complications, including epididymo-orchitis, pneumonitis and pancreatitis,' said Dr Rahul Agarwal, senior consultant internal medicine, Max-Cure Hospitals.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n******\n[2] Nepal (Kathmandu)\nDate: Fri 27 Apr 2018 10:15 am\nSource: The Himalayan Times [edited]\nhttps://thehimalayantimes.com/kathmandu/chickenpox-cases-on-the-rise-in-valley/\n\n\nAccording to Sukraraj Tropical and Infectious Disease Hospital, 3 -- 4 patients suffering from chickenpox are visiting the hospital daily.\n\n'Most of the chickenpox patients are children. Mod.JG, edited]\nhttps://www.bluradio.com/medellin/declaran-cuarentena-en-carcel-bellavista-por-brote-de-varicela-184979-ie435\n\n\nQuarantine was declared in Bellavista prison because of a varicella outbreak. The authorities are currently assessing the inmates and measures are taken, aiming to avoid additional cases.\n\nIn the last few hours, 460 inmates from ward 16 in Bellavista prison were placed into quarantine after a varicella outbreak had been detected, which led to placing 7 inmates under isolation conditions.\n\nMr. Jorge Carmona, a human rights attorney, declared that this measure had upset the inmates' relatives, since nearly 350 persons were not able to enter the facility for the Sunday visits that were originally scheduled for children and women.\n\nThe discomfort arose because the quarantine was established last [Sat 21 Jul 2018] evening, and the outbreak has been taking place for the past few days. The inmates' relatives come from many different places outside Medellin, and even from different communities. This implies they had to waste money for planning a visit to the Bellavista prison, and this could have been prevented had they been warned on time of the visit cancellation', explained Mr. Carmona.\n\nHe also emphasized that the poor hygiene conditions could be the cause for the outbreak. The authorities are currently assessing the inmates and measures are taken, aiming to avoid additional cases.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Chickenpox (Varicella) is a very contagious disease caused by the varicella-zoster virus (VZV). It causes a blister-like rash, itching, tiredness, and fever. The rash appears 1st on the stomach, back and face and can spread over the entire body causing between 250 and 500 itchy blisters. Chickenpox can be serious, especially in babies, adults, and people with weakened immune systems. The best way to prevent chickenpox is to get the chickenpox vaccine.\n\nThe virus spreads mainly by touching or breathing in the virus particles that come from chickenpox blisters, and possibly through tiny droplets from infected people that get into the air after they breathe or talk, for example. (CDC)\n\nPrisons with their closely housed inmates, provide the perfect environment to facilitate spread of the virus. Varicella-zoster virus necrotising retinitis, retinal vasculitis and panuveitis following uncomplicated chickenpox in an immunocompetent child. BMJ Case Rep. 2018. pii: bcr-2017-223823. doi: 10.1136/bcr-2017-223823; abstract available at http://casereports.bmj.com/content/2018/bcr-2017-223823.abstract\n----------------------------------------------------------------------\n\nA 4-year-old presented to Manchester Royal Eye Hospital with acute visual loss in her left eye after an uneventful episode of chickenpox.\n\nClinicians have described what they believe is only the 4th reported case of retinitis in a child following infection with chickenpox.\n\nWriting in BMJ Case Reports [see reference above], Manchester Royal Eye Hospital clinicians highlight that the case of a 4-year-old girl who presented with visual loss and redness in her left eye is a reminder that the varicella-zoster virus can cause occlusive retinal vasculitis.\n\n'Necrotising retinitis is rare in children but typically presents with acute painful unilateral visual loss,' they explain.\n\nThe authors describe how the child had a 4-day history of blurred vision in her left eye when she was referred to the hospital uveitis clinic.\n\nFour weeks previously she had mild chickenpox, which had settled, and was otherwise well.\n\nShe received combined intravenous and intravitreal antiviral treatment, leading to 'rapid improvement' with settled retinitis, no vascular occlusion, and good recovery of vision.\n\n'This case of varicella-zoster virus necrotising retinitis with panuveitis in a 4-year-old child highlights the importance of careful fundal examination in childhood uveitis, the need for urgent high-dose antiviral treatment and a multidisciplinary approach,' the authors emphasise.\n\nWithout treatment, complications of the condition can include retinal detachment, optic atrophy, contralateral eye involvement, and meningoencephalitis.\n\n[Byline: Selina Powell]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[It is noted in the BMJ publication on this rare case, that the child's recent coinfection with Epstein-Barr virus may have acted to provoke the retinitis. The reports spoke of 802 men and 695 women, affected with chickenpox.\n\nDuring the same period of 2017, only 801 cases were recorded. In other words, 696 incidents are currently reported, representing an increase of 46.5 percent.\n\nAccording to data from the National Epidemiological Bulletin, in 2017, Yucatan ended the year with total of 2802 cases of chickenpox. At a national level, the state with the highest number of patients is Veracruz, with 3379 cases. It is followed by Jalisco and the State of Mexico, with 2870 and 2603 incidents, respectively.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Maps of Mexico: http://www.ezilon.com/maps/images/northamerica/political-map-of-Mexico.gif and http://healthmap.org/promed/p/514]\n\n******\n[2] Vaccination sequelae\nDate: Thu 5 Apr 2018\nSource: MPR [edited]\nhttps://www.empr.com/news/herpes-zoster-children-varicella-vaccination-site-erythematous-papules/article/744643/\n\n\n[ref: Song H, Morley KW, Trowbridge RM, et al. Herpes zoster at the vaccination site in immunized healthy children. Pediatr Dermatol. 2018; 35(2): 230-3. doi: 10.1111/pde.13415; abstract available at https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/pde.13415]\n----------------------------------------------------------------------\nA case series published in the journal Pediatric Dermatology [see citation above] describes the occurrence of herpes zoster at the site of varicella-zoster vaccination in 7 healthy children without a history of primary varicella.\n\nEach of the 7 children received varicella-zoster vaccination between 12 and 14 months of age; the children were between the ages of 1.5 and 6 years old when they presented with symptoms. Viral exanthem, eczema herpeticum, atopic dermatitis, contact dermatitis, microcystic lymphatic malformation, herpes simplex infection, and herpes zoster infection were all considered in the differential diagnosis.\n\nIn their discussion of the case, the authors acknowledged that herpes zoster can occur in patients without a history of varicella and that it can also occur in patients after varicella vaccination, as previously reported in the literature. 'It is likely that the vaccine strain infects cutaneous sensory nerves around the injection site and travels to the dorsal ganglion through retrograde transport,' they write.\n\nAs the children in these cases did not present with typical herpes zoster rash, but rather with small pink to erythematous papules, pseudovesicles, and plaques, the authors state that a 'high index of clinical suspicion is necessary for timely diagnosis.' Direct fluorescent antibody (DFA) testing was conducted in 4 of the 7 patients.\n\nAll of the patients in this case series recovered without complications; 5 were treated with acyclovir and 2 received no treatment. 'This series supports and expands upon the existing literature regarding herpes zoster in vaccinated healthy children and highlights the close correlation between the vaccination site and cutaneous eruption,' the authors conclude.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Varicella vaccination is generally considered safe but adverse reactions have been reported, as in the cases discussed above. In addition, keratitis in association with herpes zoster virus (HZV) or varicella zoster virus (VZV) vaccines has been observed. HZV vaccination is intended for at-risk adult populations and VZV vaccination is intended for all pediatric patients. ... In most cases, the onset of keratitis occurred within days of vaccination and resolved with topical steroid eye drops and oral acyclovir. Data suggest that keratitis in association with herpes zoster or varicella vaccine is rare, is usually self-limited or resolves with treatment.\n(see Grillo AP, Fraunfelder FW. Keratitis in association with herpes zoster and varicella vaccines. Council is urging anyone with symptoms of the disease to see a doctor as soon as possible.\n\nSymptoms include an onset of slight fever, runny nose, and feeling generally unwell; and an itchy skin rash which usually appears as small lumps, turns into blisters and then scabs over a 3--4 day period.\n\nAn individual with chickenpox is contagious from the beginning of the illness - up to 2 days before the spots appear - until about 5 days after the 1st spots appear.\n\nEarly in the illness, the virus is spread by coughing and sneezing.\n\nLater in the illness, the virus is spread by direct contact with the fluid in the blisters.\n\nPeople with chickenpox should avoid others until at least 5 days after onset of the rash and up until all the blisters have dried. They should cover their nose and mouth when coughing or sneezing, dispose of soiled tissues, wash their hands well and not share eating utensils, food or drinking cups.\n\nPeople who have a past history of chickenpox and are vaccinated against the virus are less likely to contract the disease.\n\nAnyone who has not had chickenpox or been vaccinated in the past can get chickenpox.\n\nIn particular, pregnant women, newborn babies and people with a weakened immune system should avoid anyone with chickenpox and should see their doctor immediately if they have been around someone with this virus.\n\nA person diagnosed with chickenpox should rest as much as possible and avoid contact with other until no longer infected.\n\nFor more information, contact your doctor, or visit the NSW [New South Wales] Health website.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[A Healthmap/ProMED of Australia can be found at http://healthmap.org/promed/p/186 and of New South Wales, Australia, at\nhttp://healthmap.org/promed/p/284]\n\n******\n[2] USA (Louisiana)\nDate: Wed 28 Mar 2018 5:10 PM CDT\nSource: WWLTV [edited]\nhttp://www.wwltv.com/article/news/health/several-babies-at-local-daycare-center-sick-with-chicken-pox/289-532925286\n\n\nThis long Easter weekend, several parents will be staying at home taking care of very sick children, rather than going to holiday festivities.\n\nParents with babies at the Cub Corner Preschool in Lakeview are seeing something rare - a cluster of children, younger than a year old, with chicken pox.\n\n'It's very common. Chickenpox is a very contagious disease caused by the varicella-zoster virus (VZV). It causes a blister-like rash, itching, tiredness, and fever. The rash appears first on the stomach, back, and face and can spread over the entire body causing between 250 and 500 itchy blisters. Chickenpox can be serious, especially in babies, adults, and people with weakened immune systems.\n\nThe virus spreads mainly by touching or breathing in the virus particles that come from chickenpox blisters, and possibly through tiny droplets from infected people that get into the air after they breathe or talk, for example.\n\nThe best way to prevent chickenpox is to get the chickenpox vaccine. Children, adolescents, and adults should get 2 doses of chickenpox vaccine. Chickenpox vaccine is very safe and effective at preventing the disease. Most people who get the vaccine will not get chickenpox. If a vaccinated person does get chickenpox, it is usually mild -- with fewer red spots or blisters and mild or no fever. The chickenpox vaccine prevents almost all cases of severe disease.\n(Excerpted from https://www.cdc.gov/chickenpox/about/index.html). - Mod.LK\n\nHealthMap/ProMED-mail map\nBadulla, Sri Lanka: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/63890]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n Communicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Temporarily closing the University and vaccinating all unvaccinated students is probably the most effective approach to stopping spread of the virus. \n\nThe virus spreads mainly by touching or breathing in the virus particles that come from chickenpox blisters, and possibly through tiny droplets from infected people that get into the air after they breathe or talk, for example. \n\nAs such, a university official stated that a decision has been taken by the university administration to close down the university as a precaution in order to prevent the spread of the viral infection. VARICELLA UPDATE (04): SOUTH ASIA (SRI LANKA) UNIVERSITY CLOSURE\n****************************************************************\n \n\nThe best way to prevent chickenpox is to get the chickenpox vaccine.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["varicella"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-03-04 00:00:00", "2018-03-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Sri Lanka", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Badulla", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "5645052", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Varicella update (03): shingles research, India alert", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=5645052", "date": "2018-02-22 18:04:01", "main_text": "VARICELLA UPDATE (03): SHINGLES RESEARCH, INDIA ALERT\n*****************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nIn this update:\n[1] Research: shingles, children\n[2] India (Kerala): alert\n\n******\n[1] Research: shingles, children\nDate: Fri 9 Feb 2018\nSource: Outbreak News Today [edited]\nhttp://outbreaknewstoday.com/chickenpox-vaccine-linked-shingles-vaccination-site-children-15641/\n\n\nNew research in Pediatric Dermatology reports several cases of shingles that developed at the original vaccination site in healthy children after they were immunized against chicken pox. Most of these cases were initially misdiagnosed as other skin rashes. While some of these patients underwent tests to help make the diagnosis, all of the children recovered without complications.\n\nInfection with the varicella-zoster virus leads to chickenpox, or primary varicella. The virus then lies dormant and can later reactivate as shingles, or herpes zoster. The varicella-zoster vaccine is made of an attenuated live virus that prevents most people from getting chicken pox but rarely can reactivate and cause shingles.\n\nThe cases highlight the importance of recognizing shingles in vaccinated children.\n\n'Shingles in healthy immunized children is rare, but when it occurs it may correlate to the original vaccination site,' said senior author Dr. Jennifer Huang, of Boston Children's Hospital. 'Recognizing vaccine-associated shingles may prevent unnecessary procedures or testing.'\n\nCitation\n--------\nSong H, Morley KW, Trowbridge RM, et al. Herpes zoster at the vaccination site in immunized healthy children. Pediatr Dermatol. Pediatr Dermatol. 2018 Feb 6. doi: 10.1111/pde.13415 [Epub ahead of print], abstract available at:\nhttp://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/pde.13415/abstract;jsessionid=02300C02848F3F25AE7CBED39D68BA44.f03t01.\n\nAbstract\n--------\nIn this case series, we report 7 immunized healthy children without underlying immunodeficiency who presented with herpes zoster that correlated with varicella-zoster vaccination site. The morphology of the lesions included erythematous papules, pseudovesicles, and plaques, with associated pain in 2 and pruritus in 3 patients; systemic symptoms ranged from none to low-grade fevers, upper respiratory symptoms, and joint pain. These cases highlight the clinical, diagnostic, and therapeutic implications of herpes zoster in vaccinated children.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The investigators speculated that the high concentration of virus at the vaccination site may predispose the site to zoster reactivation. They also found that herpes zoster following vaccination occurred at a younger age (median age 2 vs 4 years) and shorter time from inoculation to rash onset than wild-type herpes zoster. Furthermore, the clinical presentation of herpes zoster in children is distinct, and vaccination may further modify disease presentation. The investigators noted small pink to erythematous papules or pseudovesicles, rather than true vesicles, with varying degrees of overlying hemorrhagic crust and peripheral erythema. Fever and pain are rarely prominent features, and postherpetic neuralgia is exceedingly rare in childhood cases.\n\nSee publication cited above for references and photographs of rash in these cases.\n\nThe Varicella-zoster virus also causes shingles. Chickenpox can be spread from people with shingles to others who have never had chickenpox or received the chickenpox vaccine. This can happen if a person touches or breathes in virus from shingles blisters. In these cases, a person might develop chickenpox, not shingles. Shingles cannot be passed from one person to another. (excerpted/edited CDC). - Mod.LK]\n\n******\n[2] India (Kerala): alert\nDate: Tue 20 Feb 2018\nSource: NYOOOZ, a Hindu report [edited]\nhttps://www.nyoooz.com/news/thiruvananthapuram/1038103/alert-on-chicken-pox-in-city/\n\n\nThe District Health Administration has issued an alert against chicken pox [varicella], with cases being reported regularly from many parts of the district. While no major outbreaks have been reported, the College of Engineering, Trivandrum (CET) was closed for 2 weeks on [Mon 19 Feb 2018], after 19 cases of chicken pox were reported amongst students, mostly hostel mates.\n\nAbout 13 students had been diagnosed clinically, while the rest had reported with fever and rashes, the beginning stage of the infection. The 1st case had been reported on [29 Jan 2018] and the next case on [14 Feb 2018]. Chicken pox being a highly contagious, air-borne disease, it was natural for the infection to spread in hostels, which are often congested and where students are in close contact with each other.\n\nPatients are contagious up to 5 days before and 5 days after the first rash appears. The incubation period (the time from infection to symptoms) is about 14 to 21 days after contact with the virus. Hence it is likely that there will be more cases, the District Medical Officer, P. Preetha, said.\n\nIt was just 2 weeks ago that the Sree Chitra Tirunal College of Engineering at Pappanamcode had been closed for a fortnight following a cluster of chicken pox cases. About 25-30 cases had been reported.\n\nDr. Preetha said that as summer intensifies it was usual for a number of cases to be reported every year.\n\nIn unimmunised populations, most people contract chickenpox by age 15, but all ages can contract it. Unlike before, most of the youngsters today have not been exposed to the virus as children. Chicken pox infection thus shows up more severely in adults and hence preventive vaccination is advised.\n\nHealthy persons who have not been vaccinated or not had the disease before, but who come into contact with an infected person, can lessen the severity of the infection if they get vaccinated within the first 3 to 5 days after the exposure.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Chickenpox is a very contagious disease caused by the varicella-zoster virus (VZV). When you get vaccinated, you protect yourself and others in your community. This is especially important for people who cannot get vaccinated, such as those with weakened immune systems or pregnant women.\n\nSome people who are vaccinated against chickenpox may still get the disease. Charts generated using a multi-graph tool in Gideon. https://www.gideononline.com/cases/multi-graphs/\n\n--\nSteve Berger\nGeographic Medicine\nTel Aviv Medical Center\n<steve@gideononline.com>\n\n[ProMED thanks Steve Berger for submitting these informative references on decrease in mortality following implementation of varicella vaccine.\n\nAlso see FE Andre et al. 2008. Vaccination greatly reduces disease, disability, death and inequity worldwide. Bulletin of the World Health Organization (http://www.who.int/bulletin/volumes/86/2/07-040089/en/ doi: 10.2471/BLT.07.040089). This bulletin provides an excellent summary of the benefits of vaccines. While elimination of smallpox may be the best example of a successful vaccine, data for efficacy of the measles, mumps, rubella (MMR) vaccine are given below. These vaccine-preventable diseases still occur, largely because of vaccine hesitancy, but disease severity and mortality are greatly reduced.\n\nBefore the measles vaccination program started in 1963, an estimated 3- 4 million people got measles each year in the United States. Of these, approximately 500 000 cases were reported each year to CDC; and of these, 400-500 died, 48 000 were hospitalized, and 1000 developed encephalitis (brain swelling) from measles. Since then, widespread use of measles vaccine has led to a greater than 99 percent reduction in measles cases compared with the pre-vaccine era. However, measles is still common in other countries. Unvaccinated people continue to get measles while abroad and bring the disease into the United States and spread it to others (excerpted and edited from https://www.cdc.gov/measles/vaccination.html).\n\nBefore the US mumps vaccination program started in 1967, about 186 000 cases were reported each year, but the actual number of cases was likely much higher due to underreporting. Since the pre-vaccine era, there has been a more than 99 percent decrease in mumps cases in the United States. Since the 2-dose vaccination program was introduced in 1989, mumps cases have ranged year to year from a couple of hundred to several thousand. However, in recent years, there has been an increase in the number of reported cases, from 229 cases in 2012 to 6366 cases in 2016. The recent increase has been mainly due to multiple mumps outbreaks reported across the country in settings where people often have close contact with one another, like college campuses (excerpted and edited from https://www.cdc.gov/mumps/outbreaks.html).\n\nBefore the rubella vaccination program started in 1969, rubella was a common and widespread infection in the United States. During the last major rubella epidemic in the United States from 1964-1965, an estimated 12.5 million people got rubella, 11 000 pregnant women lost their babies, 2100 newborns died, and 20 000 babies were born with congenital rubella syndrome (CRS). Once the vaccine became widely used, the number of people infected with rubella in the United States dropped dramatically. Today, [fewer] than 10 people in the United States are reported as having rubella each year. Since 2012, all rubella cases had evidence that they were infected when they were living or traveling outside the United States (excerpted and edited from https://www.cdc.gov/rubella/about/in-the-us.html).\n\nHealthMap/ProMED-mail map\nNew York, United States: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/31193\nNew York county map: http://geology.com/county-map/new-york-county-map.gif]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n \n\nBefore the US mumps vaccination program started in 1967, about 186 000 cases were reported each year, but the actual number of cases was likely much higher due to underreporting. The illness was not confirmed through laboratory analysis, but was diagnosed by the unnamed student's medical doctor. The letter, from district Superintendent Terry Ward and Cayuga County Health Department Public Health Director Kathleen Cuddy, said the student has had the chickenpox vaccine.\n\nIn a telephone interview Wed [31 Jan 2018], Ward said he heard about the case from Kathy Nodine, the high school's nurse, on the afternoon of 25 Jan. He praised Nodine, saying she suggested contacting the health department that same day. Ward said the student can return once they don't have chickenpox anymore and their chickenpox blisters have dried and crusted; as they can no longer spread the disease.\n\nCuddy said Nodine contacted people in the school community she was aware of who could experience severe effects from the disease if they were to contract it -- such as those who are pregnant or have immunodeficiency. Cuddy said she isn't aware of other probable chickenpox cases in the county. She doesn't recall hearing about another case during her 17 years as a department employee.\n\nWard used the district's new emergency phone system on Fri to send a pre-recorded message telling parents about the case. There have been no new cases reported, he said. Over the past couple weeks, Ward said, the district has been 'vigilant' during flu season, wiping down surfaces such as desktops, door knobs, keyboards and drinking fountains.\n\nWard thanked the health department for its cooperation. Cuddy praised the cooperation among the department, the district, Nodine, the student's health care provider and family. 'Everybody was on board with doing all of the ideal actions,' Cuddy said.\n\nAccording to the district's letter, chickenpox can be transmitted through people touching the saliva, blisters and mucus of an infected person and through the air by sneezing and coughing. Touching a newly contaminated item, such as clothing, can spread it as well. Touching the blisters of someone with shingles can also cause chickenpox in someone who has not been vaccinated and has never had the disease.\n\nSomeone with chickenpox can spread it 1 -- 2 days before getting the rash, until all of the person's blisters scab over, typically from 5 -- 7 days. It normally takes a person up to three weeks to develop chickenpox after being exposed to someone with it.\n\nInitial symptoms can include a sudden slight fever and feeling weak, with an itchy blaster-like rah following afterward. Chickenpox blisters are more commonly found on body parts typically covered as opposed to exposed parts.\n\nIt is recommended that those who have never been vaccinated for chickenpox or were vaccinated once, consult their health care provider about additional appropriate vaccination.\n\n[Byline: Kelly Rocheleau kelly.rocheleau@lee.net]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from Healthmap alerts\npromed@promedmail.org\n\n\n\n\n\n[Doctors generally diagnose chickenpox based on its characteristic rash. If there's any doubt about the diagnosis, chickenpox can be confirmed with laboratory tests, including blood tests or a culture of lesion samples. Varicella (chickenpox) vaccine is highly effective. One dose of vaccine prevents 95% of moderate disease and 100% of severe disease. Two doses of vaccine increases effectiveness. If given to those who are not immune within 5 days of exposure to chickenpox, the vaccine prevents most cases of disease.\n\nA Healtmap of United States can be found at http://healthmap.org/promed/p/106 and New York State, United States, at http://healthmap.org/promed/p/234 -Mod LK\n\n]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n \n\nSomeone with chickenpox can spread it 1 -- 2 days before getting the rash, until all of the person's blisters scab over, typically from 5 -- 7 days. Sources said all children in the grip of the disease were in the age group of 6-12 years.\n\nThe Health Department said that according to the survey and case history, the 1st case of chickenpox was reported on [11 Nov 2017]. The child was sent back to his native place in Saharanpur.\n\nA team of the Health Department after screening the students on [Mon 11 Dec 2017] found that 13 students out of 41 enrolled in the madarsa were infected with the disease. The affected students are 6 to 12 year old children.\n\nA teacher from the madarsa took them to the Civil Hospital, Banur, and came to know that the students were suffering from chickenpox. The hospital authorities soon informed the Civil Surgeon at Mohali and then the information was passed on to the district epidemiologist, who reached the area with a rapid response team.\n\nMadarsa principal Mohammad Shahzad said, 'All affected students were discharged from the Civil Hospital after getting supportive treatment and have been shifted to a separate room as a precautionary measure.'\n\nHarpreet Oberoi, SMO, Banur, said, 'The children had complained of redness, itchy rashes on the skin spreading to almost everywhere on the body. But no case of high fever was reported following which they were discharged after the treatment.'\n\nRita Bhardwaj, Civil Surgeon, Mohali, told The Tribune that she had sent a team comprising district epidemiologists and health workers to the school.\n\nMeanwhile, a 4-year-old girl from Peer Muchalla reportedly suffering from chickenpox was brought to the CHC Dhakoli on [Tue 12 Dec 2017].
