Nearly 3/4ths [75 percent] of them did not receive a vaccination against the virus, and an additional 14 percent did not receive the recommended number of doses, according to the health department, which says 2 doses of the vaccine are 98 percent effective.\n\nThe city sent out an alert to physicians reminding them to make sure children are up to date with their vaccinations.\n\nThe city in September [2015] urged Orthodox schools and parents to vaccinate their children after a whooping cough outbreak.\n\nSome yeshivas have the lowest reported vaccination rates among schools in the city, according to state data.\n\nIn 2013, the city health department reported 48 cases of measles in the Orthodox Jewish community residing in Borough Park and Williamsburg [neighborhoods in Brooklyn, New York City].\n\n[Byline: Dan Goldberg]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Vaccines work on the theory of herd immunity, i.e., that disease outbreaks can be prevented if a population has a high proportion of people who are immune to a given disease. Large numbers of people who opt out of vaccination lower a population's level of herd immunity. 'If you have a community where the herd immunity is very, very low and also if there's crowding or very, very large families ... then the disease could spread rapidly,' C Gill [Associate Professor at Boston University's Center for Global Health & Development and a specialist in infectious diseases and vaccines] said.\n\nSince the mid 1990s -- when the vaccine became mandatory for children entering public grade school -- chickenpox rates have predictably plummeted. According to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, the vaccine prevents 3.5 million cases, 9000 hospitalizations, and about 100 deaths caused by the illness every year. And despite the ongoing ruckus made by anti-vaccination activists, 90 percent of children from the ages of 19 to 35 months have gotten their 1st dose of the vaccine as of 2010. The 2nd dose is recommended at 4 to 6 years old.\n\nWhether a similar anti-vaccination sentiment explains the current outbreak is difficult to say, however. Prominent rabbis within more restrictive sects of the Orthodox community have spoken out against vaccination, and a Brooklyn measles outbreak in 2013 centered around 2 Orthodox Jewish communities in the neighborhoods of Borough Park and Williamsburg.\n\nBut other voices in the community have loudly rebuffed these vaccine skeptics, and there's seemingly no Jewish law that discourages vaccination. In 2013, a representative from the New York City Department of Health and Mental Hygiene told Jewish publication Forward that 96 percent of Brooklyn students who attended yeshivas, the traditional form of education for Orthodox children, had received their necessary shots. More likely than not, then, the outbreak is the result of an isolated pocket of resistance.\n\nBecause chickenpox is highly contagious, though, health officials are advising medical providers throughout the city to stay on high alert. And to make sure that their patients and staff are fully up to date on their own shots. While the chickenpox vaccine isn't foolproof, 2 doses are about 90 percent effective at preventing the disease. People suspected of contracting chickenpox but who haven't yet shown any symptoms can also be given the vaccine up to 3 days after they were 1st exposed.\n\n(From http://www.rawstory.com/2016/05/chickenpox-outbreak-hits-nyc-orthodox-jewish-community-mostly-in-unvaccinated-kids/)\n\nA map of Williamsburg, New York can be accessed at http://www.aaccessmaps.com/images/maps/us/ny/brooklyn_n/brooklyn_n.gif. A HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at http://healthmap.org/promed/p/745. - Mod.LK\n\n******\n[2] India (New Delhi)\nDate: Sat 14 May 2016\nSource: Hindustan Times [edited]\nhttp://www.hindustantimes.com/delhi/warm-weather-brings-chicken-pox-to-delhi-s-air/story-a2pkNgIYaFE0ULP5E86tMN.html\n\n\nCity [New Delhi] doctors have been getting a flurry of chickenpox cases for a month now, with the soaring [temperatures].\n\n'I have already seen about 25-30 cases of chickenpox since past few weeks. The warm temperature that we see these days helps in activating viruses and bacteria that attack people, especially with low immunity,' says Dr Srikant Sharma, senior consultant, department of medicine, Moolchand Hospital.\n\nPeople at high risk are those with underlying medical conditions such as diabetes, blood pressure, cancer, chronic kidney ailment, HIV or tuberculosis and people on steroids or other immunity-suppressing medicines.\n\nNormally, red-coloured rashes appear within 24 hours of a person getting fever, up to 102 Fahrenheit, which turn into fluid-filled blisters, before finally crusting over. The blisters are itchy and take about 10 days to crust over. A person develops relative immunity toward chickenpox for life.\n\n'The virus lies dormant. There may be a local infection at some point that makes skin to flare up, but the disease per se happens only once in a life time,' said a doctor from the paediatrics department, All India Institute of Medical Sciences.\n\nThe chickenpox virus Varicella-zoster uses droplets in the air to travel. If a person with the infection coughs or sneezes near a healthy person, he or she might contract it.\n\nA person may also contract the infection if exposed to the fluid from the eruptions.\n\nChickenpox is a common childhood disease. But when people catch the disease for the 1st time as adults, the symptoms tend to be severe.\n\nEarlier there was no specific treatment for chickenpox, but these days certain anti-viral drugs are available that minimise its magnitude significantly. The disease is mostly treated at the symptomatic level, to reduce severe itchiness and to bring down fever.\n\nDue to its highly contagious nature, doctors advise people to stay away from those who have developed rashes on skin, usually within 24 hours of getting fever.\n\nThe vaccine Varicella costs about Rs 1500 [about USD 22] and is the widely used protection against chickenpox. However, due to its high cost, doctors feel it is not feasible for everyone to use it. 'It's not part of the national immunisation programme purely because of its high cost and also the disease normally is not of very serious nature. The vaccine is good for those who can afford it, otherwise it should be given mainly to high-risk people,' said Dr Sharma.\n\nAccording to the doctors, it takes about 2-3 days to develop immunity against the disease, hence, it is important to take the vaccine shot immediately after a near one has developed symptoms.\n\nSymptoms of chickenpox\n----------------------\n- A rash that usually begins on the body and face and later often spreads to the scalp and limbs\n- The rash is often itchy\n- It begins as small red spots which develop into blisters in a couple of hours\n- After a couple of days, the blisters turn into scabs\n- New blisters may appear after 3-6 days\n- High grade fever\n- Severe head and body ache\n- Loss of appetite\n- Children have mild symptoms\n- In children, the symptoms last 7-10 days, and longer in adults.\n- Adults get severe symptoms and take longer to recover. They are also more likely than children to suffer from complications.\n\n[Byline: Rhythma Kaul]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[A map of the National Capital Territory of Delhi, India can be accessed at http://healthmap.org/promed/p/330. - Mod.LK]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n Communicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Vaccines work on the theory of herd immunity, i.e., that disease outbreaks can be prevented if a population has a high proportion of people who are immune to a given disease. They must have a medical record of the diagnosis, or the children must have a blood test to verify that they have had the disease. The virus spreads easily from people with chickenpox to others who have never had the disease or been vaccinated. The virus spreads mainly by touching or breathing in the virus particles that come from chickenpox blisters, and possibly through tiny droplets from infected people that get into the air after they breathe or talk, for example.\n\nA person with chickenpox can spread the disease from 1-2 days before they get the rash until all their chickenpox blisters have formed scabs (usually 5-7 days). It takes about 2 weeks (from 10 to 21 days) after exposure to a person with chickenpox for someone to develop chickenpox. If a person vaccinated for chickenpox gets the disease, they can still spread it to others.\n\nVZV causes a blister-like rash, itching, tiredness, and fever. The rash appears first on the stomach, back and face and can spread over the entire body causing between 250 and 500 itchy blisters. Chickenpox can be serious, especially in babies, adults, and people with weakened immune systems.\n\nFor most people, getting chickenpox once provides immunity for life. However, for a few people, they can get chickenpox more than once, although this is not common.\n\nThe best way to prevent chickenpox is to get the chickenpox vaccine. Chickenpox vaccine is very safe and effective at preventing the disease. Most people who get the vaccine will not get chickenpox. If a vaccinated person does get chickenpox, it is usually mild, with fewer red spots or blisters and mild or no fever. The chickenpox vaccine prevents almost all cases of severe disease.' - Mod.LK\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/225.]", "summary": "Communicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Excerpted/edited from the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention:\n'Chickenpox is a very contagious disease caused by the varicella-zoster virus (VZV). VARICELLA, SCHOOL CHILDREN - USA (NORTH CAROLINA) (02)\n Date: Thu 7 Dec 2006\n From: Prof. Steve Berger <mberger@post.tau.ac.il>\n --\n **\n \n \n", "reports": [{"diseases": ["varicella"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2006-12-25 00:00:00"], "locations": []}]}, {"archive_id": "4067633", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Varicella-zoster vaccine-associated keratitis: RFI", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=4067633", "date": "2016-03-03 20:59:07", "main_text": "VARICELLA-ZOSTER VACCINE-ASSOCIATED KERATITIS: REQUEST FOR INFORMATION\n**********************************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\nDate: Wed 2 Mar 2016\nSource: Pharmacy Times [edited]\nhttp://www.pharmacytimes.com/news/watch-for-keratitis-when-administering-varicella-vaccine\n\n\nPharmacists [and other healthcare professionals] should watch for signs of keratitis after administering the varicella vaccine for chickenpox and shingles.\n\nResearchers from the University of Missouri School of Medicine recently discovered a link between the vaccine and corneal inflammation in rare cases. In light of this finding, patients with a history of eye inflammation should consult a health care provider before getting vaccinated against varicella zoster virus, they advised. After studying more than 12 million case reports from national and international registries, the researchers uncovered at least 20 cases of keratitis in children and adults that occurred within a month of varicella vaccine administration. The symptoms of keratitis developed within 24 days of vaccination among the adults and within 14 days among the children.\n\n'The vaccine is a live virus, and in rare, susceptible individuals, this can cause a reaction in the eye in the form of herpes-zoster keratitis,' Frederick W. Fraunfelder, MD, MBA, director of the National Registry of Drug-Induced Ocular Side Effects and chair of the Department of Ophthalmology at the MU School of Medicine, explained to Pharmacy Times.\n\nAccording to Dr. Fraunfelder, pharmacists should keep the following things in mind when administering the varicella vaccine.\n\n1. Ask questions about eye health history before administering the vaccine.\n\nDr. Fraunfelder said pharmacists should ask patients the following 2 questions:\n- Do you have a history of zoster or shingles in or around the eye(s)?\n- Do you have a past ocular history of herpes virus affecting your eyes?\n\n2. Keep in mind that keratitis can occur in patients with no prior eye-related problems.\n\n'Most of the keratitis cases reported [in the study] occurred without any prior eye-related events. This is especially true in the pediatric age group who get chickenpox vaccines,' Dr. Fraunfelder noted.\n\n3. Counsel patients about the rare risk of varicella vaccine-related keratitis.\n\nPharmacists should inform patients about the rare risk of a zoster-keratitis reaction, especially those who have experienced it in the past.\n\n'It is helpful to be informed, and if a reaction occurs, [the patient] probably shouldn't get the vaccine ever again,' Dr. Fraunfelder said.\n\n4. Look for eye symptoms after vaccination.\n\n'Blurred vision, red eye, cloudy cornea, and light sensitivity are some warning signs that can start appearing within days to weeks after administration,' Dr. Fraunfelder said.\n\n5. If keratitis symptoms present, refer the patient to an ophthalmologist.\n\n'Referral to an ophthalmologist is important, as the long-term sequelae of the condition can be minimized with treatment,' Dr. Fraunfelder explained. 'After that, a subspecialist would administer steroids and/or antivirals.'\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The study, 'Varicella and Zoster Vaccine-associated Keratitis,' was presented at the 2015 meeting of the American Academy of Ophthalmology in Las Vegas. It is not clear from the information available if the corneal inflammation is due to direct infection of the live but attenuated vaccine virus or from an immunological event related to the virus or one not related to the virus but exacerbated by the immunization. ProMED would like more information regarding the pathophysiology here. - Mod.LL]\n\n.................................................sb/ll/je/dk", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n Communicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The study, 'Varicella and Zoster Vaccine-associated Keratitis,' was presented at the 2015 meeting of the American Academy of Ophthalmology in Las Vegas. The vaccine is a live virus, and in rare, susceptible individuals, this can cause a reaction in the eye in the form of herpes-zoster keratitis,' Frederick W. Fraunfelder, MD, MBA, director of the National Registry of Drug-Induced Ocular Side Effects and chair of the Department of Ophthalmology at the MU School of Medicine, explained to Pharmacy Times. It is not clear from the information available if the corneal inflammation is due to direct infection of the live but attenuated vaccine virus or from an immunological event related to the virus or one not related to the virus but exacerbated by the immunization. \n\nPharmacists should inform patients about the rare risk of a zoster-keratitis reaction, especially those who have experienced it in the past. \n\n'Most of the keratitis cases reported [in the study] occurred without any prior eye-related events. \n\nDr. Fraunfelder said pharmacists should ask patients the following 2 questions:\n- After studying more than 12 million case reports from national and international registries, the researchers uncovered at least 20 cases of keratitis in children and adults that occurred within a month of varicella vaccine administration. \n\n'Blurred vision, red eye, cloudy cornea, and light sensitivity are some warning signs that can start appearing within days to weeks after administration,' Dr. Fraunfelder said. In light of this finding, patients with a history of eye inflammation should consult a health care provider before getting vaccinated against varicella zoster virus, they advised. However, in a follow up email, a reference to the disease smallpox -- eradicated in 1979 -- was deleted.\n\nClasses were rescheduled at Nalsar Hyderabad in January 2015 and at NLU Jodhpur in February 2015 due to the outbreak of swine flu on campus.\n\n[Byline: Prachi Shrivastava]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The primary herpes virus infection caused by the varicella-zoster virus is formally referred to as varicella, not chickenpox. A 10-day hiatus may not be adequate for the incubation period. - Mod.LL\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/323.]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n Communicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[The primary herpes virus infection caused by the varicella-zoster virus is formally referred to as varicella, not chickenpox. \n\nAn email was sent to students and faculty on campus today [8 Feb 2016] informing everyone of the 10-day break, Bhatt said, and he added that the campus and hostels will be fumigated during the student body's absence. The school was closed for the week to monitor and treat affected students.\n\nThis pustulovesicular rash represents a generalized herpes outbreak due to the Varicella-zoster virus (VZV) pathogen/CDC.\n\nLast week, the Ministry of Health screened over 400 students from the St. George's Anglican School. The exercise saw a team of qualified nursing personnel conducting screening and evaluation of students at the St. George's Anglican Junior and Senior Schools.\n\nA total of 255 and 183 students from the junior and senior schools respectively were examined on the compound as the institution reopened its doors following the completion of sanitation and cleaning operations.\n\nDuring the screening/examination at the school, several students with skin lesions, fever, cold, headaches and skin rashes were identified and were not given clearance for a return to the classroom. Some of the students were referred to the doctor, and others were requested to be observed at home.\n\nMeanwhile, the ministry will undertake a daily monitoring of the school, conduct education sessions with teachers and undertake another medical evaluation of students, in particular the ones that are to be observed at home and referred.\n\nChickenpox is a common, usually benign childhood disease caused by the varicella-zoster virus (VZV), a member of the herpes family. This virus causes 2 distinct diseases; varicella (chickenpox) is the primary infection, and later when VSV reactivates, herpes zoster (shingles).\n\nChickenpox is highly contagious and is spread by coughing and sneezing, by direct contact and by aerosolization of the virus from skin lesions. You can also get it by contact with the vesicle secretions from shingles.\n\nThe disease is characterized by fever and a red, itchy skin rash that usually starts on the abdomen, back or face and then spreads to nearly all parts of the body. The rash begins as small red bumps that appear as pimples or insect bites. They then develop into thin-walled blisters that are filled with clear fluid which collapse on puncture. The blisters then break, crust over, and leave dry brown scabs.\n\nThe chickenpox lesions may be present in several stages of maturity and are more abundant on covered skin rather than exposed. Lesions may also be found in the mouth, upper respiratory tract and genitals.\n\nChickenpox is contagious from 1-2 days before the rash forms and continues until all the lesions are crusted over (usually about 5 days).\n\nThis disease is more serious in adults than in children. Complications of chickenpox are rare, but include pneumonia, encephalitis and secondary bacterial infections.\n\nInfection with this virus usually gives lifelong immunity, although 2nd attacks have been documented in immunocompromised people. The viral infection remains latent, and disease may recur years later as shingles.\n\nAccording to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC), the chickenpox vaccine is the best protection against chickenpox. The vaccine is made from weakened varicella virus that produces an immune response in your body that protects you against chickenpox. The chickenpox vaccine was licensed for use in the United States in 1995.\n\n[Byline: Robert Herriman]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[A map of Grenada can be found at http://healthmap.org/promed/p/34. - Mod.LK]\n\n******\n[2] Varicella - USA (North Carolina) schoolchildren\nDate: Mon 7 Mar 2016\nSource: Charlotte Observer [edited]\nhttp://www.charlotteobserver.com/living/health-family/article64604577.html\n\n\n[A] 5-year-old doesn't have chickenpox. But 5 other elementary students in Union County do. And because [the 5-year-old] hasn't been vaccinated against the childhood illness, she is one of 15 children who have been ordered by the county health director to stay out of school, in quarantine, for 21 days.\n\n[The 5-year-old]'s mother, of Indian Trail, is confused and angry about the health department's decision. She questions why her child -- and the other healthy, unvaccinated children -- should be sent home when the vaccination didn't prevent the other children from getting sick.\n\n'There's nothing wrong with her, but she can't come back to school for 21 days,' [she] said. 'The only ones with the virus are the ones that got the vaccination.'\n\nHedrick said her daughter, a kindergartner at Poplin Elementary, 'can go to the public library. She can go to the grocery store. We can go out to eat. But she cannot go to school. We are not talking about Ebola. We are not talking about the plague. We are talking about chickenpox.'\n\nThe 5 children with chickenpox, also called varicella, attend Poplin and Hemby Bridge elementary schools, and all had been vaccinated, said Union County Health Director Phillip Tarte.\n\n'The very nature of isolation in this instance is to remove the most vulnerable away from the disease,' Tarte told the Observer in an email. Although the vaccine is not 100 percent effective, he said those who get the vaccine and then get chickenpox 'have a greater level of protection to avoid complications.'\n\nIn justifying the quarantine, Tarte quoted the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention: 'Children who lack evidence of immunity and whose parents refuse vaccination should be excluded from school from the start of the outbreak through 21 days after rash onset of the last identified case.'\n\nUnlike measles and mumps, chickenpox is not required to be reported to health officials, so there are no good statistics on the number of cases. In Mecklenburg County, Dr. Stephen Keener, the Health Department's medical director, said he hasn't had a report of chickenpox in the past year and has never ordered a quarantine of healthy children during a chickenpox outbreak.\n\nKeener did order a quarantine in late 2014 after a case of measles was reported. The quarantine orders went to a few people who had direct contact with the measles victim and who could not produce documentation of vaccination.\n\nChickenpox, caused by the varicella-zoster virus, is highly contagious and spreads by direct contact or through the air by coughing or sneezing. It takes 10 to 21 days to develop symptoms after being exposed to someone with the disease. Recovery from chickenpox confers long lasting natural immunity.\n\nMany cases of chickenpox are mild, but deaths can occur from the disease. The vaccine was licensed for use in the United States in 1995. Before that, about 100 people died every year in the United States from the disease. The vaccine has also been linked with injuries. Since creation of the federal Vaccine Injury Compensation Program, it has received a total of 125 claims -- 9 deaths and 116 serious injuries -- related to the chickenpox vaccine. Compensation was awarded in 71 cases, and 28 were dismissed.\n\nThe mother said she declined to get her daughter vaccinated against chickenpox and other childhood illnesses because her older child, now 8, had bad reactions -- from ear infections to drug-resistant pneumonia -- after receiving vaccines as a small child. 'We went through a very traumatic time with him,' she said.\n\n{The 8-year-old] received vaccinations only until he was 2. After that, [his mother] got religious exemptions that allow both children to attend school without having received all the mandatory childhood vaccines.\n\nKeeping [the 5-year-old] at home has created challenges for the family. [the mother], who works for Wells Fargo, has arranged to work from home and help her with homework until the quarantine is over on 17 Mar 2016. She has also hired a tutor for [the child], who is in the school's Spanish immersion program.\n\n[Byline: Karen Garloch]\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail from HealthMap Alerts\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Chickenpox is caused by the varicella-zoster virus. Note, it is not a pox virus. The virus is an airborne pathogen that spreads easily; one can get it from an infected person who sneezes, coughs, or shares food or drinks, or if one touches the fluid from a chickenpox blister. A person who has chickenpox can spread the virus even before he or she has any symptoms.\n\nThe disease is vaccine preventable, although the vaccine is 70-90% effective. Vaccinated individuals who come down with the disease are generally protected from the most severe symptoms.\n\nThe vaccine was licensed in the US in 1995. Prior to that, in the early 1990s, an average of 4 million people got chickenpox; 10 500-13 000 were hospitalized (range, 8000 to 18 000), and 100 to 150 died each year.\n\nThe chickenpox vaccine carries less risk of severe disease than does getting the disease, and most people who get the vaccine do not have any problems with it. Nonetheless, as stated in the report, rare side effects may occur. Serious side effects to the chickenpox vaccine are very rare and more often are evident after the 1st dose than after the 2nd one.\n\nAccording to the CDC, possible reactions include: soreness, redness, or swelling where the shot was given, fever, mild rash or several small bumps after vaccination, as well as seizure (jerking and staring spells) that may be caused by fever. Seizures after chickenpox vaccination may or may not be related to chickenpox vaccine.\n\nSerious side effects from chickenpox vaccine are extremely rare, but may include severe brain reactions and low blood count.\n\nThe issue raised by this report, i.e., prohibiting unvaccinated healthy children from attending school following evidence of varicella cases in the school, is a difficult one and very controversial. This practice is recommended by the CDC to protect those most vulnerable. As noted from the numbers above, the vaccine -- even though not 100% effective -- apparently has greatly decreased the number of cases. The exact incidence is unknown, though, since varicella is not a reportable disease. Certainly, vaccination is the most effective protection available against serious disease. But where vaccination of schoolchildren is voluntary and not mandatory, the situation described in this report will have to be confronted every time cases occur in a classroom setting.\n\nFor more information on the vaccine, see http://www.cdc.gov/vaccines/vpd-vac/varicella/basic-side-effects.htm, and for information on the disease, see http://wonder.cdc.gov/wonder/prevguid/p0000108/p0000108.asp#head001000000000000.\n\nA map of North Carolina, USA can be found at http://healthmap.org/promed/p/235. - Mod.LK\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/106.]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n \n\nChickenpox is a common, usually benign childhood disease caused by the varicella-zoster virus (VZV), a member of the herpes family. \n\nChickenpox, caused by the varicella-zoster virus, is highly contagious and spreads by direct contact or through the air by coughing or sneezing. \n\nChickenpox is contagious from 1-2 days before the rash forms and continues until all the lesions are crusted over (usually about 5 days). \n\nThe 5 children with chickenpox, also called varicella, attend Poplin and Hemby Bridge elementary schools, and all had been vaccinated, said Union County Health Director Phillip Tarte. \n\nChickenpox is highly contagious and is spread by coughing and sneezing, by direct contact and by aerosolization of the virus from skin lesions. \n\nSerious side effects from chickenpox vaccine are extremely rare, but may include severe brain reactions and low blood count. All patients spent at least 3 days in separate shelters together with other people.\n\n1st case: A 17-year-old male, history of contact with his younger brother who had been diagnosed with chickenpox. He is currently in a shelter together with another approximately 19 people (including children). He is being treated on an outpatient basis in our hospital.\n\n2nd case: A 6-year-old girl, no history of contact with chickenpox suspected/confirmed cases in the shelter. She was diagnosed with chickenpox based on clinical signs and symptoms. She is currently being treated on an outpatient basis in our hospital.\n\n3rd case: A 26-year-old male visited the emergency department of our hospital a week ago where he was diagnosed with chickenpox. He was treated with the antiviral drug (acyclovir) and is now almost recovered from his illness.\n\nSome medical doctors who have visited other shelters also found additional chickenpox cases in Kathmandu, indicating that chickenpox could potentially spread among people, especially to those who had not had been previously infected or immunized, because the virus is highly contagious and spreads quickly and easily through the respiratory route.\n\n--\nDr. Sher Bahadur Pun\nSukraraj Tropical and Infectious Disease Hospital\nKathmandu, Nepal\n<drsherbdr@yahoo.com>\n\n[ProMED thanks Dr. Pun for this firsthand report.\n\nThe incubation period of varicella is 14-21 days, averaging about 17 days, so these individuals were exposed prior to the earthquake.\n\nIn a 1991 report on sero-epidemiology in Nepal (Kubo T, Rai SK, Nakanishi M, Yamano T: Seroepidemiological study of herpes viruses in Nepal. Southeast Asian J Trop Med Public Health. 1991;22:323-325), only 25 percent of children 1-4 years old showed antibodies to VZV, but the number of positives increased rapidly with age (82.9 percent in greater than 15 years age). This would suggest that most Nepali adolescents and adults (a cohort with higher risk of more severe illness from varicella) are likely to be immune, but overcrowding in shelters would enhance the likelihood of increased cases. - Mod.LL\n\nA HealthMap/ProMED-mail map can be accessed at: http://healthmap.org/promed/p/139.]", "summary": "\n\n--\nDr. Sher Bahadur Pun\nSukraraj Tropical and Infectious Disease Hospital\nKathmandu, Nepal\n<drsherbdr@yahoo.com>\n\n[ProMED thanks Dr. Pun for this firsthand report. Torres <torresj@iname.com>\nSource: El Universal Mexico online, Tue 16 Mar 2004 (translated by\nMaria Jacobs, ProMED-ESP) [edited]\n<http://www.el-universal.com.mx/pls/impreso/noticia.html?id_nota=210705&tabla=notas>\n\nMexico: Outbreak of Varicella (Chickenpox) in Yucatan State\n--------------------------------------------------\nAn outbreak of varicella [chickenpox] that so far has affected more\nthan 1000 persons, both children and adults, has placed the health\nauthorities of the state of Yucatan on alert, as they face the risk\nof acute dissemination of the disease over the coming weeks.\nJose Pereira Carcano, Health Secretary, indicated that to date, 1060\ncases of the disease have been reported throughout the state, a\nnumber more than 100 percent higher than that registered during the\nsame period in 2003.\nIn addition, over the past few weeks, health officials have detected\nrotavirus infection in some 30 people as well as 8 cases of dengue\nfever, one of which was of the haemorrhagic type. These findings have\ncontributed to the concern expressed by health authorities.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n An outbreak of varicella [chickenpox] that so far has affected more\nthan 1000 persons, both children and adults, has placed the health\nauthorities of the state of Yucatan on alert, as they face the risk\nof acute dissemination of the disease over the coming weeks. Jose Pereira Carcano, Health Secretary, indicated that to date, 1060\ncases of the disease have been reported throughout the state, a\nnumber more than 100 percent higher than that registered during the\nsame period in 2003. In addition, over the past few weeks, health officials have detected\nrotavirus infection in some 30 people as well as 8 cases of dengue\nfever, one of which was of the haemorrhagic type. At Jonesville Elementary school, almost 60 of\nthe school's 480 students have been diagnosed with or displayed\nsymptoms of chickenpox over the past month. The exact cause of the\nchild's death has not been determined, but local health officials say\nthey doubt chickenpox is to blame. The classic symptoms of this\nhighly contagious disease are blisters that break out and scab over\nafter a few days. Oftentimes, the blisters are preceded by a headache\nand fever.\nAccording to the school's principal, the outbreak isn't limited just\nto students who haven't been vaccinated. 'We have had both kids who\nhave had vaccination and those that haven't to have the chickenpox,'\nsaid principal Rick Swaim.\nAcross the state, the current class of kindergartners was the 1st\nrequired by law to be vaccinated for chickenpox. In September [2006],\nthe state issued a recommendation urging parents to get a 2nd booster\nshot. Because the disease appears to be so widespread -- affecting\nnot just Jonesville, but also West Yadkin and Booneville elementary\nschools -- the Yadkin County Health Department has sent out a letter\nto notify parents and doctors.\nAll over North Carolina, parents are being urged to make sure their\nchildren have both the vaccine and the booster. Health officials say\nthat since state law doesn't require chickenpox cases to be recorded,\nthey don't know whether 60 cases in one county in one month is odd.\n--\nBrent Barrett\nIndianapolis, IN, USA\n<salbrent@sbcglobal.net>\n[According to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC)\nwebsite, chickenpox-related deaths happened in about 100 people a\nyear before 1995, prior to introduction of an improved vaccine. The\nCDC states that about 40 percent of deaths due to chickenpox happen\nin children younger than age 10.\nResearch results indicate the current vaccine is about 85 percent\neffective in preventing an outbreak. Unfortunately, the absence of a\nrequirement in North Carolina to record chickenpox cases makes\ninterpretation of this extensive outbreak affecting 3 elementary\nschools in Yadkin County difficult to interpret. Possible reasons are\ninadequate vaccine coverage, delay in implementation of universal\nvaccination, requirement for more than a single immunization to\nachieve sufficient protection.\nComprehensive accounts of chickenpox\n<http://www.cdc.gov/nip/diseases/varicella/>\nand chickenpox vaccination\n<http://www.cdc.gov/nip/menus/vaccines.htm#varicella>\nare available at the CDC website.\nA map of the counties comprising the state of North Carolina is\navailable at\n<http://geology.com/state-map/north-carolina.shtml>. Yadkin County\nlies inland towards the northwest of the state. - Mod.CP]", "summary": "A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n \nParents and school officials are on alert as dozens of children in 3\nYadkin County elementary schools are being treated for chickenpox\n(varicella), and one 8-year-old has died while recovering from a mild\ncase of the disease. [According to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC)\nwebsite, chickenpox-related deaths happened in about 100 people a\nyear before 1995, prior to introduction of an improved vaccine.", "reports": [{"diseases": ["varicella"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2021-09-25 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "in", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Yadkin County", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "North Carolina", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "West Yadkin", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Jonesville", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2187726", "headline": "PROMED: Varicella, neonatal? (5)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2187726", "date": "1996-01-12 23:50:00", "main_text": "VARICELLA, NEONATAL (5)\n=======================\n[1]\nFrom: csanders <csanders@healthnet.zw>\nDate: Thu, 11 Jan 96 14:24:10 +0200\nAccording to 'Dermatology and Venereology' (Braun-Falco ed.),\nvaricella infection in the first trimester of pregnancy may lead\nto congenital abnormalities in about 1% of the cases. There appears\nto be no such risk in the second and third trimester. If a maternal\nvaricella occurs at the end of pregnancy about 25% of\nthe neonates will develop clinical symptoms. The course the infection\ntakes is more severe in the infant, the closer the maternal infection\nis to the delivery date. Pregnant women with a documented exposure and\nwithout acquired immunity should receive VZIG [varicella-zoster immune\nglobulin], which is useless after the manifestations of varicella have\nappeared. In the above case it was warranted to give the neonate\nVZIG at birth which hopefully has modified the clinical symptoms.\n--\nCornelus J.G. Sanders M.D. * Health Services Department * PO Box 1946 *\n32 Weir Avenue, Hillside * Bulawayo City * Zimbabwe * Tel: 263 9-75011/\n63207; 42090 (home) * Fax: 263 9-65534 * E-mail: csanders@healthnet.zw *\n.....................................\n[2]\nFrom: Jeff Snedeker <snede001@lightlink.com>\nDate: Thu, 11 Jan 1996 23:13:05 -0500 (EST)\n\nRegarding the newborn with suspected varicella, the description of the\ninfant's lesions as maculopapular (as opposed to vesicular) and their\ndisappearance within 24 hours would be highly unusual for an infant with\nvertically-acquired varicella. Are you sure these skin lesions weren't\nerythema toxicum (a common rash of newborn which may appear pustular and\nis often mistaken for lesions of Herpes simplex virus)? If any\nlesions remain the distinction can be made easily by doing a\nWright's or Giemsa stain on the contents of the pustules;\nerythema toxicum lesions contain eosinophils, while VZV [varicella]\nlesions should contain multinucleated giant cells and PMNs.\nI presume the VZIG [varicella zoster immune globulin] was given\nto the infant for prophylaxis rather than as treatment, since\na sick newborn with VZV infection should receive acyclovir.\nWith regard to the relative timing of the infant's versus the mother's\nlesions, several possibilities exist. The infant can acquire virus either\npostnatally (i.e. horizontally) from contact with the mother, in utero\nduring the mother's secondary viremia (when her lesions appear), or, much\nless commonly, in utero during the mother's primary viremia (during the\nincubation period). In the first instance, the infant's lesions usually\nappear about 2 weeks postnatally; in the second instance, the infant's\nlesions usually appear about 2 weeks after the mother's lesions first\nappeared; in the third instance, the infant's lesions appear at about the\nsame time or shortly after the mother's. In the case in question, one\nwould have to suppose that the third route of exposure was operative,\nassuming of course that the infant indeed has varicella.\nI hope mother and baby are both doing well.\n--\nJeff Snedeker, MD\nPediatric Infectious Diseases\nNortheast Pediatrics\nIthaca, NY\ne-mail: snede001@lightlink.com\n.....................................\n\n[3]\nFrom: MARGARET CURRAN <MARGARET.M.C.CURRAN@HHLGCS.ausgovhhcs.telememo.au>\n(Tel 06 289 7416 mdp 15)\n\nRegarding neonatal varicella:\nAnother useful reference on this subject is:\nGilbert GL. Chickenpox during pregnancy. (4)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2187725", "date": "1996-01-12 23:50:00", "main_text": "VARICELLA, NEONATAL (4)\n=======================\n[1]\nFrom: 'Nancy A. Lass, M.D.' <nlass@midway.uchicago.edu>\nDate: Thu, 11 Jan 1996 09:22:38 -0600 (CST)\n\nVaricella is indeed a significant infection in the newborn infant. The\nvaricella zoster virus is a known human teratogen. Fetuses exposed in utero\nmay exhibit skin lesions, limb abnormalities, eye abnormalities including\ncataracts, chorioretinitis or blindness, brain atrophy, mental retardation,\nand death. Those infants who are exposed in utero and delivered before the\ntransfer of significant maternal antibody are at risk for severe infection\nand a 20-30% mortality rate (this would include infants whose mothers\ndeveloped the disease within 5 days prior to delivery or within 2 days\nafter; generally refers to infants developing a rash between the first 5-10\ndays of life). Infants developing a rash in the first 5 days usually have\nreceived some maternal antibody and have a less severe course. Those\ninfants developing the varicella rash after 10 days generally are felt to\nhave acquired the disease after birth and also tend to do better.\nGenerally, an infant born to a mother who has developed varicella within 5\ndays of delivery should receive varicella zoster immune globulin (VZIG) 125\nunits within 48 hours of delivery, preferably as soon as possible. This\nwill not prevent neonatal varicella, but will modify its course. Acyclovir\nmay also modify the course, but should not be necessary if VZIG has been\ngiven. Isolation of the mother and baby may be necessary depending on\nwhether mother is in incubation period, baby is in a neonatal intensive care\nunit, etc.\nFurther information can be obtained from the following texts:\n'Neonatal-Perinatal Medicine', Fanaroff and Martin, eds. Fifth Edition,\nMosby-Year Book Publishers, 1992\n'Infections of the Fetus and Newborn Infant', Remington and Klein, eds., Third\nEdition, W.B. Saunders Publisher\n1994 Red Book: Report of the Committee in Infectious Diseases, 23rd\nEdition, Published by and available from the American Academy of Pediatrics,\nElk Grove Village, IL 60009\n--\nNancy A. Lass, M.D.\nAssistant Professor of Pediatrics and Clinical Pharmacology\nSection of Neonatology, Department of Pediatrics\nUniversity of Chicago\ne-mail: nlass@midway.uchicago.edu\n.............................................\n[2]\nFrom: brnbaum@unixg.ubc.ca (David Birnbaum)\nIn addition to standard texts, you might try Paryani SG et al., Intrauterine\nInfection with Varicella-Zoster Virus after Maternal Varicella, N ENGL J MED\n1986;314(24):1542-6, and the subsequent letter by by Preblud SR et al., N\nENGL J MED 1986;315(22):1416-7. These references, located by searching the\nPARADIGM database, summarize a prospective case series and meta-analysis.\n--\nDavid Birnbaum, PhD, MPH\nClinical Assistant Professor\nDept. of Health Care & Epidemiology\nUniversity of British Columbia\ne-mail: brnbaum@unixg.ubc.ca\n.............................................\n\n[3]\nFrom: 'Peters, C.J.' <cjp0@ciddvd1.em.cdc.gov>\nDate: Thu, 11 Jan 96 10:12:00 EST\nThere's a case report of varicella in a pregnant woman that goes through\nsome of the considerations, as well. (2)\n========================\nAccording to Abram S. Benenson, ed., Control of Communicable\nDiseases Manual, 'Neonates developing varicella between ages 5 and 10 days\nor those whose mothers develop the disease 5 days prior to or within 2 days\nafter delivery are at increased risk of developing severe generalized\nchickenpox with up to a 30% mortality rate.'\n--\nJack Woodall, Ph.D., FAS ProMED List Moderator\nNew York State Dept.of Health, Albany NY, USA\ne-mail: jack.woodall@wadsworth.org\nhttp://www.healthnet.org/promed.html\n..........................................................................\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. (3)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2187724", "date": "1996-01-12 23:50:00", "main_text": "VARICELLA, NEONATAL (3)\n=======================\n[Replies on this topic through Friday are collected here and in two\nadditional postings. Replies received were also forwarded\nthis morning directly to the inquirer. Sincere thanks to all the\nsubscribers who replied so promptly and with a wealth of information. - Mod.]\n\n[1]\nDate: Thu, 11 Jan 1996 12:57:17 GMT-10\nFrom: Richard Doherty <Richard.Doherty@med.monash.edu.au>\nConventional wisdom is that infants born between five days before and\ntwo days after a mother comes out in a varicella rash are at risk of\nsevere varicella, because they have been exposed in-utero without\nhaving remained there long enough to benefit from the emerging\nmaternal immune response. Whether maternal immunoglobulin has anything\nto do with it is not clear to me given the short time frame and\nthe fact that IgM traditionally does not cross the placenta.\nI would interpret the appearance of the baby's rash at that point as\nbeing indicative of transplacental spread of VZV [varicella-zoster\nvirus] during the initial low level viraemic phase which occurs\nat about three days after initial infection (it is this low level wave\nthat you are trying to block with the neutralising antibody in\nV-ZIG [varicella-zoster immune globulin] when you give it to exposed at risk\nindividuals). Spread at this stage would seem to be a little unusual,\nbut by no means inconceivable. If this is so, then the baby may\nwell have been exposed to VZV for long enough to benefit from whatever\nspecific or non specific responses might have been transferred\ntrans-placentally, but perhaps more importantly, might have also been\nable to mount a response itself.\nThese postulates notwithstanding, I would have a very low threshold\nfor starting this infant on intravenous acyclovir, and would have expect\nV-ZIG to have little if any effect on the clinical course of the\nillness.\nTwo references from a quick search are appended, but the chapter on\nVZV infection in Remington and Klein, 'Infectious Diseases of the\nFetus and Newborn Infant' is likely to give you all the info you need\nand all the key references.\n89343356\nOutcome in newborn babies given anti-varicella-zoster immunoglobulin after\nperinatal maternal infection with varicella-zoster virus.\nMiller E; Cradock-Watson JE; Ridehalgh MK\nLancet (ENGLAND) Aug 12 1989 2 (8659) p371-3 ISSN: 0140-6736\n281 newborn babies whose mothers had chickenpox and 25 whose mothers had\nherpes zoster during the perinatal period were investigated. IgG antibody was\npresent at birth in all babies born more than 7 days after the onset of\nmaternal chickenpox. When the mother's rash appeared 7-3 days before delivery\nprogressively fewer babies were born with antibody, and no infant born less\nthan 3 days after the onset had antibody at birth. Infants were given 100 or\n250 mg of anti-varicella-zoster immunoglobulin (VZIG) shortly after birth or\nthe onset of post-natal maternal chickenpox. 1 infant died, without neonatal\nvaricella. Of the 280 surviving infants 169 (60%) were infected--134 (48%)\nwith chickenpox and 35 (13%) without clinical features. The clinical attack\nrate was highest (60%) in infants whose mothers had chickenpox between 7 days\nbefore and 7 days after delivery. Chickenpox was severe in 19 infants, 16 of\nwhom were exposed to maternal chickenpox between 4 days before and 2 days after\ndelivery. There was no evidence that a severe outcome was associated with\ntransplacentally acquired infection. Perinatal maternal herpes zoster did not\ncause neonatal infection. VZIG should be given to infants at risk, including\nthose whose mothers have chickenpox during the last 7 days of pregnancy.\n\n89132297\nVaricella-zoster virus infections in pregnancy [see comments]\nFox GN; Strangarity JW\nAm Fam Physician (UNITED STATES) Feb 1989 39 (2) p89-98 ISSN: 0002-838X\nNote: Comment in: Am Fam Physician 1989 Aug;40(2):55\nVaricella-zoster virus can cause a distinct congenital syndrome, a\npotentially fatal neonatal infection and life-threatening maternal illness.\nPhysicians can reduce morbidity from these conditions by advising nonimmune\npregnant women to avoid exposure to chickenpox and herpes zoster and, when\nindicated, by promptly administering varicella-zoster immune globulin. When\nprevention fails, acyclovir may be effective therapy. (24 References)\nBest wishes\n--\nRichard Doherty, Professor of Paediatrics\nDepartment of Paediatrics, Faculty of Medicine, Monash University\nMonash Medical Centre, Clayton, VIC 3168.\nPhone Int'l +61 3 9550 4490 Fax Int'l +61 3 9550 4124\ne-mail: Richard.Doherty@med.monash.edu.au\n...........................................\n[2]\nFrom: 'Dykewicz, Clare' <cad3@NIP1.EM.CDC.GOV>\nDate: Thu, 11 Jan 96 10:59:00 EST\nNeonatal varicella may be biphasic. Infants may have a few lesions, show\nimprovement for several days, then develop a generalized rash. Some\nnewborns may develop prolonged varicella with new lesions occurring into the\nsecond week of life. Possible complications of neonatal varicella include\nbacterial superinfection, and pneumonia. About 30% of newborns born to\nmothers who develop varicella 5 days before, or 2 days after delivery will\ndie of neonatal varicella. Death typically occurs from pneumonia or\ndisseminated visceral involvement. VZIG [varicella-zoster immune\nglobulin] administration can decrease morbidity and mortality.\nThe continued temperature instability of this child is concerning,\nand may signify ongoing viral or bacterial infection.\nClose observation is warranted. Depending on the clinical\ncourse, intravenous acyclovir may be indicated.\n--\nClare Dykewicz\nChief, Mumps, Rubella, Varicella Activity\nNational Immunization Program, CDC\ne-mail: cad3@NIP1.EM.CDC.GOV\n.............................................................................\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Send\ncommands to subscribe/unsubscribe, get archives, help,\netc. to: \nClare Dykewicz\nChief, Mumps, Rubella,", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "mumps", "rubella", "varicella"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["1996-01-11 00:00:00", "1989-03-25 00:00:00", "2021-03-26 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Remington", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Clayton", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "4910", "headline": "PRO/EDR> Varicella, Asian Games - Qatar ex Maldives", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=4910", "date": "2006-11-29 00:00:00", "main_text": "VARICELLA, ASIAN GAMES - QATAR EX MALDIVES\n******************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\nDate: Wed 29 Nov 2006\nFrom: Brent Barrett <salbrent@sbcglobal.net>\nSource: Mail and Guardian online, SAPA-Agence France Presse report [edited]\n<http://www.mg.co.za/articlepage.aspx?area=/breaking_news/breaking_news__sport/&articleid=291658>\n\nQatar: Asian games hit by chickenpox outbreak\n-----------------------------------------------\nThe Asian Games have been hit by a chickenpox outbreak amongst the\nMaldives team, but the organizers insist that the problem will not\naffect the 10 000 athletes taking part. [The 15th Asian Games are\nbeing held in Doha, Qatar from 1 to 15 Dec 2006. The Maldives\nvolleyball team was scheduled to participate in a pre-Games\nqualifying round. - Mod.CP]\nThe disease hit 3 members of the Indian Ocean [Maldives] island's\nvolleyball squad. Dr. Abdullah Al-Mussleh, the Games program manager\nfor health care and doping control, said that the disease was 1st\nspotted in one player shortly after his arrival in Doha. 'He was\nimmediately isolated, and all of the national Olympic committee of\nthe team concerned was screened for chickenpox. None of them showed\nany indication of the disease at that stage,' said Dr. Al-Mussleh.\nEight of the team were immune from the disease, but 7 were not. The\nwhole team has since been isolated, and all are on anti-viral\nmedicine. On Tuesday [28 Nov 2006], 2 more Maldives volleyball\nplayers developed chickenpox rash, and they have also been isolated\nat the city's main Hamad Hospital. 'One has chickenpox; the other has\na high possibility of having chickenpox,' added Dr. Al-Mussleh.\nAs a result of the outbreak, the team was forced to cancel its match\nagainst Taiwan on Tuesday [28 Nov 2006]. But the Olympic Council of\nAsia (OCA) has said that the team will be free to resume their\ncampaign as volleyball is not a contact sport.\n--\nBrent Barrett\nIndianapolis, IN, USA\n<salbrent@sbcglobal.net>\n[Varicella (chickenpox) is caused by Human herpesvirus 3 (formerly\nknown as Varicella-zoster virus) resulting in febrile illness in\nchildren and occasionally adults, and herpes-zoster (shingles/hives)\nin adults. Chickenpox is a common childhood illness, with an\nincubation period of about 2 weeks, with a characteristic rash and\nmild fever. In adults, illness can be more severe, with a painful\nrash developing centripetally on the face and trunk. The rash becomes\nvesicular and eventually resolves.\nThe virus is spread by the respiratory route and in some individuals\nbecomes latent in a dorsal route ganglion. Reactivation of infection\noccurs predominantly to the elderly and immune-compromised.\nIn temperate climates, chickenpox is primarily a disease of\nchildhood, but there is some evidence that the age of onset may be\nlater in the Indian sub-continent. Consequently, there may be some\nenhanced risk of transmission of this virus among participants in the\nAsian Games in Doha, notwithstanding the fact that volleyball is not\na contact sport.\nA map of Qatar showing the location of Doha can be accessed at\n<http://geography.about.com/library/cia/blcqatar.htm>.\n- Mod.CP]\n[This outbreak is being posted as an alert as the participants in\nthese athletic games include members from 45 countries (Afghanistan,\nBahrain, Bangladesh, Bhutan, Brunei, Cambodia, China, Taiwan, North\nKorea, Hong Kong, Indonesia, India, Iran, Iraq, Japan, Jordan,\nKazakhstan, Kuwait, Kyrgyzstan, Laos, Lebanon, Macau, Malaysia,\nMaldives, Mongolia, Myanmar, Nepal, Oman, Pakistan, Palestinian team,\nPhilippines, Qatar, South Korea, Saudi Arabia, Singapore, Sri Lanka,\nSyria, Tajikistan, Thailand, East Timor, Turkmenistan, United Arab\nEmirates, Uzbekistan, Vietnam, and Yemen). Hence, the threat of\nimportation of cases to other countries is high. The incubation\nperiod is approximately 7-21 days, and participants returning to\ntheir home countries might very well be within the incubation period\nfor clinical illness onset upon after return to their homes. While\nchickenpox (varicella) is usually a benign disease, it is one of the\npredisposing illnesses that have been associated with Reyes syndrome,\na serious and at times fatal encephalopathy seen in children\nfollowing viral infections. It is also mistaken for smallpox\n(variola) and has resulted in false alarms of possible variola\noutbreaks when the affected cases included older age groups. - Mod.\nMPP]", "summary": "[This outbreak is being posted as an alert as the participants in\nthese athletic games include members from 45 countries (Afghanistan,\nBahrain, Bangladesh, Bhutan, Brunei, Cambodia, China, Taiwan, North\nKorea, Hong Kong, Indonesia, India, Iran, Iraq, Japan, Jordan,\nKazakhstan, Kuwait, Kyrgyzstan, Laos, Lebanon, Macau, Malaysia,\n A ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n While\nchickenpox (varicella) is usually a benign disease, it is one of the\npredisposing illnesses that have been associated with Reyes syndrome,\na serious and at times fatal encephalopathy seen in children\nfollowing viral infections. \nBrent Barrett\nIndianapolis, IN, USA\n<salbrent@sbcglobal.net>\n [Varicella (chickenpox) is caused by Human herpesvirus 3 (formerly\nknown as Varicella-zoster virus) resulting in febrile illness in\nchildren and occasionally adults, and herpes-zoster (shingles/hives)\nin adults.
